Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Dispute over Term in Salam and Distinction from Mudaraba
Analyzing whose claim is accepted in salam term disputes and deriving a general qa'idah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts the muslim ilayh's claim regarding the term in a salam dispute and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument based on analogy to mudaraba.

Core Idea
Denying a beneficial term is stubbornness; the denier's word is rejected.

In a salam contract, if the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies that a repayment term was agreed, a dispute arises: should the seller's claim that a term existed be accepted? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on this procedural question, the Sahibayn's objection using analogy to mudaraba, and the general legal principle derived by the author of al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Whose claim is accepted in salam when the term (ajal) is denied? The rabb al-salam denies the term, while the muslim ilayh affirms it.
The term is a condition of valid salam.
2
State the Hanafi position
The muslim ilayh's claim is accepted because the rabb al-salam's denial is contradictory to the initial acknowledgment of the contract and is stubborn.
Acknowledging the contract implies accepting all its conditions.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn analogize salam to mudaraba, where the owner's denial of profit is accepted. They claim the same should apply here.
Their qiyas: both involve a forward payment and a dispute over a benefit.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Two key differences: (1) In mudaraba, an invalid contract changes to hire; in salam, invalid salam remains salam. (2) Mudaraba is non-binding (ja'iz), so dispute dissolves the contract; salam is binding (lazim), so the dispute does not dissolve, making the denier a claimant of invalidity and thus stubborn.
Hence the qiyas is invalid due to difference in 'illa.
5
Derive the general qa'idah
If one's speech denies something beneficial for oneself, that denial is rejected; the other party's statement is accepted. If one denies something harmful, there is disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
This qa'idah is extracted from the specific ruling on salam.

مدعی یعنی مضارب کے پاس چونکہ بینہ موجود نہیں اس لیے منکر یعنی رب المان کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور عقد سلم چونکہ لازم ہے اس لیے رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ کے اختلاف سے عقد سلم مرتفع نہ ہو گا اور جب عقد سلم السلام معت ہوگا اور متعنت کا قول چونکہ مردود ہوتا ہے اور اس کے ساتھی کا قول اور مقبول ہوتا ہے اس لیے رب السلم کا قول مردود اور مسلم الیہ کا قول مقبول اور معتبر ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس پوری تقریر سے ایک قاعدہ کلیہ مستنبط ہوا وہ یہ کہ اگر کسی کا کلام تعنت اور سرکشی پرمشتمل ہو بایں طور کہ اس چیز کا انکار کرتا ہو جو اس کے لیے نافع اور سودمند ہے تو باتفاق احناف اس کا قول مردود اور اس کے ساتھی کا کلام معتبر ہوگا ۔ اور اگر کسی کا کلام خصوصیت کے طور پر ہو بایں طور کہ اپنے لئے نقصان دہ چیز کا منکر ہو اور دونوں ایک ہی عقد پر متفق ہوں تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک جو حت عقد کا مدعی ہے اس کا قول قبول ہوگا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک منکر کا قول قبول ہے اگر چہ وہ صحت ہی کا منکر ہو۔
Chapter of Salam. He can do so when he has acknowledged the term and time; so the owner of salam, having acknowledged the validity of salam, then denied it. And denial after acknowledgment is rejected; therefore the owner of salam's denial of validity after acknowledging the term is rejected and unacceptable. And when the owner of salam's denial of the term is not accepted, then the statement of the muslim ilayh who claims the term will be accepted. You can also say this: the owner of salam and the muslim ilayh undertook to conclude the salam contract, and when a person undertakes a contract, he commits to all its conditions, and the term is one of the conditions of salam sale. So their undertaking the contract is an acknowledgment of the validity of salam, but when the owner of salam denied the term, it is as if he denied after acknowledgment, and denial after acknowledgment is rejected by Shariah, so the owner of salam's denial of the term will not be considered and accepted. And when the owner of salam's statement denying the term is not considered, then the muslim ilayh's claim of the term will be considered. Unlike mudaraba... etc. is the answer to the analogy of the two companions. The gist of the answer is that analogizing salam contract to mudaraba contract is not correct because there is a great difference between them. The first difference is that in mudaraba, in case of dispute, the two parties do not agree on a single contract. Because the owner of capital who claimed exclusion of ten dirhams, he is claiming that the mudaraba is invalid, and the mudarib who denied that is claiming the mudaraba is valid. And when mudaraba is valid, it is called a partnership contract, and when invalid, it is called hire. In contrast, in salam contract, despite the dispute, both the owner of salam and the muslim ilayh agree on a single contract; only that the denier of the term claims the salam is invalid, and the one affirming the term claims the salam is valid. The gist is that salam sale, even if invalid, remains salam and does not become another contract, while mudaraba, if invalid, does not remain mudaraba but becomes hire. The second difference is that mudaraba contract is not binding, because after the mudaraba contract, both the mudarib and the owner have the option to rescind, while salam contract is binding; thus, rescission by one party does not rescind it; mutual consent is required. So when there is such a difference between salam and mudaraba, how can analogizing salam to mudaraba be correct? As for why the owner's statement is accepted in mudaraba, the answer is that since mudaraba is non-binding, the dispute between owner and mudarib causes the contract to cease. And when the contract ceases, their dispute is not considered; only the mudarib's claim of profit in the owner's capital remains. The mudarib claims he has profit, the owner denies. Since the mudarib has no evidence, the denier's statement is accepted. And since salam is binding, the dispute does not cause the contract to cease. So the owner of salam who denies the term is effectively claiming the salam's invalidity. And the one claiming invalidity is stubborn and rebellious because there is no benefit in denying the term. Therefore, the owner is stubborn, and the stubborn person's statement is rejected, while his companion's statement is accepted. So the owner's statement is rejected and the muslim ilayh's statement is accepted and considered. The author of Hidayah says that from this entire discussion a general principle is derived: that if someone's speech is based on stubbornness and rebellion, such that he denies something that is beneficial for him, then by consensus of the Hanafis his statement is rejected and his companion's statement is accepted. And if someone's speech is specific in that he denies something harmful for himself and both agree on one contract, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the statement of the one who claims the contract's validity is accepted, and according to the two companions the statement of the denier is accepted even if he is denying validity.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whose claim is accepted in a salam contract when the rabb al-salam (buyer) denies that a term (ajal) was stipulated, while the muslim ilayh (seller) affirms it. The text first establishes the general rule: since the rabb al-salam originally acknowledged the contract (by initiating it), his later denial of the term is contradictory and therefore rejected. The muslim ilayh's claim of the term is accepted because it aligns with the contract's validity. The author then contrasts salam with mudaraba to refute an analogy by the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Two key differences are highlighted: (1) In mudaraba, a dispute changes the contract's nature (valid = partnership, invalid = hire), while in salam, an invalid salam remains salam. (2) Mudaraba is non-binding (ja'iz), so the dispute dissolves the contract, leaving only a profit claim where the denier's statement prevails due to lack of evidence. Salam is binding (lazim), so the dispute does not dissolve it; the denier of the term is considered stubborn (muta'anni) since denying a beneficial term harms him, thus his statement is rejected. Finally, a general qa'idah is derived: if a party's speech involves stubborn denial of a beneficial thing, their statement is rejected and the other's accepted. In a specific scenario where one denies a harmful thing and both agree on one contract, Imam Abu Hanifa accepts the claimant of validity (since it aligns with the contract), while the Sahibayn accept the denier (since he is not stubborn).

Key Terms
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract who pays the price in advance.
مسلم الیہ (muslim ilayh): The seller in a salam contract who receives the price and is obligated to deliver the goods later.
اجل (ajal): The fixed term or delivery date in a salam contract.
مضاربت (mudaraba): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits.
تعنت (ta'annut): Stubbornness or obstinacy, especially denying something beneficial without reason.
قاعدہ کلیہ (qa'idah kulliyyah): A general legal principle derived from the specific ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general qa'idah derived in the passage, what is the ruling when a party's statement involves stubborn denial of something beneficial to himself?
A) The denier's statement is accepted because he has the right to deny any condition.
B) The denier's statement is rejected, and the other party's statement is accepted.
C) The contract becomes void.
D) The matter is referred to an expert to determine the truth.

Key Takeaway
Denying a beneficial term in a binding contract is stubborn; the denier's claim is rejected, and the affirmant's claim is accepted, establishing a general principle that rejects ta'annut.

Conditions for Salam in Cloth: Length, Width, and Weight for Silk
Specifying the required descriptions for valid salam in cloth and silk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid salam in cloth and explain why weight is necessary for silk according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Salam in cloth requires length, width, and thickness; silk adds weight because its value depends on weight.

This lesson examines the conditions for salam (advance sale) in cloth as stated by Imam al-Quduri. The passage distinguishes between ordinary cloth and silk, and highlights a key difference between the Hanafi school and other madhahib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Salam is a forward sale where price is paid upfront for future delivery. The subject matter must be fully specified to avoid uncertainty (gharar).
Focus on the conditions for the object, not the contract formation.
2
State the general rule for cloth
For ordinary cloth (thiyab), salam is valid when length, width, and thickness (ruq'a) are specified. This makes the cloth known (ma'lum) and deliverable.
Thickness refers to fineness or texture (not weight).
3
Apply the basis for validity
The underlying principle is that the subject matter must be known and capable of delivery. Specifying these dimensions eliminates uncertainty.
This aligns with general salam conditions from Hadith.
4
Identify the exception for silk
For silk cloth (thawb harir), weight must also be stated because weight is intended (maqsud) and affects value significantly.
Omission of weight leads to jahalah (uncertainty) and invalidates the contract.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Imams Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad do not require weight for silk; they consider length and width sufficient. The Hanafi school requires weight.
The practical difference: Hanafis rule salam invalid without weight; others consider it valid.

Source Text

کپڑوں میں بیع سلم کا حکم
قال ويجوز السلم في الثياب اذا بين طولا وعرضا ورقعة لانه اسلم فى معلوم مقدور التسليم على ما ذكرنا وان كان ثوب حرير لابد من بیان وزنه ايضا لانه مقصود فيه
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا کہ کپڑوں میں سلم جائز ہے جبکہ ان کا طول، عرض اور موٹا باریک ہونا بیان کر دیا ہو کیونکہ اس نے ایسی معلوم چیز میں بیع سلم کی ہے جس کا سپر د کر نا قدرت میں ہے جیسا کہ ہم ذکر کر چکے۔ اور اگر ریشمی کپڑا ہوتو اس کا وزن بیان کرنا بھی ضروری ہے کیونکہ ریشم میں وزن بھی مقصود ہوتا ہے۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر سوتی کپڑے کا طول ، عرض اور موٹا، باریک ہونا بیان کر دیا ہو تو اس کی بیع سلم جائز ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں کپڑ امعلوم ہو جاتا ہے اور اس کا سپرد کر نا ممکن ہے اور سابق میں گذر چکا ہے کہ شئی معلوم، مقدور التسلیم کی بیع سلم جائز ہوتی ہے اس لیے اس کپڑے کی بیع سلم بھی جائز ہوگی۔ اور اگر کپڑا ریشم کا ہو تو صرف طول عرض بیان کرنا کافی نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کا وزن بیان کرنا بھی ضروری ہے کیونکہ ریشم کے کپڑے میں طول و عرض کے ساتھ اس کا وزن بھی مقصود ہوتا ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ وزن کے کم یا زیادہ ہونے سے ریشم کے کپڑے کی قیمت مختلف ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس جب وزن بھی مقصود ہے تو اس کا بیان کرنا بھی ضروری ہوگا حضرت امام مالک، امام شافعی اور امام احمد کے نزدیک وزن بیان کرنا شرط نہیں ہے۔
Ruling on salam in cloth. He (al-Quduri) said: Salam in cloth is permissible when its length, width, and thickness are specified, because he has made salam in a known item that can be delivered, as we have mentioned. And if it is a silk cloth, it is necessary to state its weight as well, because weight is intended in it. Translation: Al-Quduri said that salam in cloth is permissible when their length, width, and thickness are stated, because he has made salam in a known item that is deliverable, as we have mentioned. And if it is a silk cloth, stating its weight is also necessary because weight is also intended in silk. Commentary: Sahib Quduri states that if the length, width, and thickness of a cotton cloth are stated, then its salam is permissible because in that case the cloth becomes known and deliverable, and it has passed earlier that salam in a known, deliverable item is permissible. Therefore, salam in this cloth is also permissible. And if the cloth is silk, then merely stating length and width is not sufficient; stating its weight is also necessary because in silk cloth, weight is also intended along with length and width. This is why the price of silk cloth varies with weight. So when weight is also intended, its statement is necessary. According to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad, stating weight is not a condition.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Quduri's matn on salam in cloth, expanded by the commentator. The ruling states that salam is valid in cloth (thiyab) when its length (tul), width (ard), and thickness (ruq'a) are specified. This makes the cloth known (ma'lum) and deliverable (maqdur al-taslim), fulfilling the conditions of salam. For silk cloth (thawb harir), an additional condition is required: the weight must be stated because the value of silk depends significantly on weight. The commentator notes that this is a Hanafi position; the other three imams do not require weight specification. The reasoning is that silk is sold by weight, and omitting it would cause uncertainty (gharar).

Key Terms
ثیاب (thiyab): Cloth or garments; in this context, fabric sold by length and width.
طول (tul): Length of the cloth.
عرض (ard): Width of the cloth.
رقعة (ruq'a): Thickness or quality of the cloth (literal: patch; here means texture/fineness).
ثوب حریر (thawb harir): Silk cloth.
وزن (wazn): Weight, a measure to eliminate uncertainty in silk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what additional condition is required for salam in silk cloth compared to cotton cloth?
A) The color must be specified.
B) The weight must be specified.
C) The thread count must be specified.
D) The place of manufacture must be specified.

Key Takeaway
Salam in silk requires weight specification because weight is intended (maqsud) and essential for avoiding uncertainty.

Salam in Jewels and Pearls: The Distinction of Weight
Ruling on salam in jewels and pearls with an exception for weight-based sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in jewels and pearls and distinguish between cases where salam is valid and invalid based on the mode of sale (weight vs. count).

Core Idea
Salam in jewels and large pearls is invalid due to excessive variation; small pearls sold by weight are valid because weight removes uncertainty.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school regarding salam (forward sale) in valuable items such as jewels and pearls. The general principle is that the subject of salam must be precisely known (ma'lum). Items that vary excessively in value among individual units, like jewels or large pearls, cannot be adequately described for a valid salam contract. However, small pearls that are sold by weight, being standardized, are considered known and thus permissible. This ruling illustrates how the Hanafi school operationalizes the condition of knowledge (ilm) in salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage addresses two separate acts: salam in jewels and large pearls (invalid) and salam in small pearls sold by weight (valid). Clearly state each ruling.
The ruling uses the term 'la yajuz' (not permissible) for the first case and 'yajuz' (permissible) for the second.
2
Extract the underlying cause (illah)
The prohibition in jewels and large pearls is due to excessive variation (tafawut fahish) among individual units, making them unknown. This violates the condition that the subject of salam must be ma'lum (known).
The concept of 'tafawut fahish' is key to Hanafi reasoning on gharar.
3
Recognize the exception
Small pearls sold by weight are excepted because weight standardizes the units, eliminating the excessive variation and making them known. Identify the condition that triggers validity: sale by weight.
The exception is explicitly tied to the method of sale (wazn).
4
Apply the general principle
The ruling is derived from the broader condition that the subject of salam must be ma'lum. Weight is one means of achieving this knowledge. Consider other measures (e.g., volume, number with uniformity) that could also satisfy this condition.
This principle extends to other commodities in salam transactions.
5
Connect to real-world application
This distinction prevents gharar (uncertainty) in forward sales. As a mufti or legal advisor, you must examine whether the commodity is sold by weight/count/volume and whether individual units vary excessively.
The ruling applies to both precious stones and other items with similar variation.

یا قوت اور موتیوں میں بیع سلم جائز نہیں
ولا يجوز السلم في الجواهر ولا في الخرز لان أحادها تتفاوت تفاوتا فاحشا وفي صغار اللؤلؤ التي تباع وزنا يجوز السلم لأنه مما يعلم بالوزن
ترجمہ اور یا قوت اور موتی میں بیع سلم جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان کے افراد میں بہت بڑا تفاوت ہوتا ہے۔ اور چھوٹے چھوٹے موتی جو وزن سے فروخت ہوتے ہیں (ان میں بھی ) سلم جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ وزن سے معلوم ہو جاتے ہیں۔ تشریح مسئلہ، جواہر اور موتی چونکہ عددی ہیں اور مالیت کے اعتبار سے ان کے افراد میں بہت بڑا تفاوت ہوتا ہے اس لیے ان کی بیچ سلم جائز نہیں ہے اور چھوٹے چھوٹے موتی جو وژن سے فروخت ہوتے ہیں مثلاً جن کو سرمہ اور دوا میں ڈالا جاتا ہے ان کی بیع سلم وزن سے جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ وزن سے معلوم ہو جاتے ہیں اور شئی معلوم کی مسلم چونکہ جائز ہوتی ہے اس لیے ان کی بیع سلم بھی جائز ہو جائے گی۔
Salam in jewels and pearls is not permissible. And salam is not permissible in jewels (jawahir) nor in pearls (khraz) because their individual units differ greatly. And in small pearls that are sold by weight, salam is permissible because they become known by weight. Translation: Salam in jewels and pearls is not permissible because their individuals have great variation. And in small pearls that are sold by weight, salam is permissible because they become known by weight. Commentary: Since jewels and pearls are countable and their individuals have great price variation, salam in them is not permissible. And small pearls that are sold by weight, for example those used in antimony and medicine, salam by weight is permissible because they become known by weight, and salam in a known item is permissible, so salam in them is also permissible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on salam in valuable stones and pearls. The general rule: salam is invalid in jewels (jawahir) and pearls (khraz) because individual pieces vary greatly in value (tafawut fahish). This variation makes them unknown (majhul), violating the condition that the subject of salam must be precisely known. However, an exception is made for small pearls sold by weight (sighar al-lu'lu' allati tuba'u waznan). Since these are standardized by weight, they become known, and salam in them is valid. The commentator gives the example of pearls used as medicinal ingredients (sorma/dawa). The reasoning is consistent with the general principle that salam is valid in items that are known and deliverable.

Key Terms
جواہر (jawahir): Precious stones or jewels, individually distinct and highly variable in value.
خرز (khraz): Large pearls (often used as beads). Different from small pearls (lu'lu').
لؤلؤ (lu'lu'): Pearls; small pearls sold by weight are valid for salam.
وزن (wazn): Weight as a measure to standardize and eliminate uncertainty.
تفاوت فاحش (tafawut fahish): Excessive variation in value among individual units.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Salam in items with excessive individual variation is invalid unless the items are standardized by weight or similar measure that makes them known (ma'lum).

Salam in Bricks: Ruling and Rationale
Ruling validating salam in bricks with mold specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity condition for salam in bricks and explain the illah of uniformity (adadi mutaqārib).

Core Idea
Salam in bricks is valid because they are uniform once the mold is specified.

Bricks may seem like ordinary construction materials, but their classification in salam (forward sale) hinges on a precise legal principle: uniformity. In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school that permits salam in both unbaked (labin) and baked (ājur) bricks provided the mold is specified. The reasoning (ta'līl) is that bricks are 'countable and relatively uniform' (adadi mutaqārib), so specifying the mold eliminates uncertainty and makes the subject matter sufficiently known.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and subject
Identify that the ruling is about salam in labin (unbaked brick) and ājur (baked brick). The hukm is that it is permissible (lā ba'sa / no harm).
2
Identify the condition
The condition for validity is that a 'known mold' (malban ma‘lūm) must be specified. The mold determines size and shape, making the bricks identifiable.
Without specifying the mold, the contract may be invalid due to uncertainty.
3
Locate the illah (reason)
The author gives the reason: 'because they are relatively uniform in number (adadi mutaqārib)'. This means individual bricks do not differ significantly in value when the mold is fixed.
This is a ta'līl — the operative cause for permissibility.
4
Understand the legal principle
Salam is valid in items that can be accurately described. For countable items that are uniform (adadi mutaqārib), description by number and mold is sufficient.
Bricks are contrasted with jewels (adadi mutafāwīt) where salam is not valid without further specification like weight.
5
Apply to similar cases
Consider other construction materials like cement blocks or tiles. If they are produced using a fixed mold, the same ruling applies.
The principle extends to any item that is uniform in value after specifying its form.

Source Text

کچی پکی اینٹ میں بیع سلم اس وقت جائز ہے جبکہ سانچہ معلوم ہو
ولا بأس بالسلم فى اللبن والآجر اذا سمى ملبنا معلوما لانه عددى متقارب لا سيما اذا
ترجمہ اور کچی اور پکی اینٹوں میں بیع سلم کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے جبکہ کوئی معلوم سانچہ بیان کر دیا گیا ہو ۔ کیونکہ اینٹ عددی متقارب ہے خصوصاً جب سانچہ بیان کر دیا ہو۔
تشریح مسئلہ، اینٹیں بیچی ہوں یا پکی ہوں اگر ان کا سانچہ متعین کر دیا گیا ہو تو ان کی بیع سلم جائز ہے کیونکہ اینٹیں عددیات متقار بہ میں سے ہیں یعنی مالیت میں ان کے افراد متفاوت نہیں ہوتے اور عددی متقارب کی بیع سلم چونکہ جائز ہے اس لیے اینٹوں کی بیع سلم بھی جائز ہو گی بشرطیکہ ان کا سانچہ متعین کر دیا گیا ہو ۔
Salam in Unbaked and Baked Bricks is Permissible When the Mold is Known
And there is no harm in salam in unbaked bricks (labin) and baked bricks (ājur) when a known mold is specified, because they are relatively uniform in number (countable and close), especially when the mold is specified.
Translation: And there is no harm in salam in unbaked and baked bricks when a known mold is stated, because bricks are relatively uniform in number, especially when the mold is stated.
Explanation: The issue: Whether bricks are unbaked or baked, if their mold is specified, salam in them is permissible, because bricks are among the relatively uniform countable items, i.e., their individual units do not differ in value, and salam in relatively uniform countable items is permissible, so salam in bricks is permissible provided their mold is specified.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage addresses salam in construction materials: unbaked (labin) and baked (ājur) bricks. The ruling is that salam in bricks is valid when a specific mold (shape and size) is designated. The reason is that bricks are 'countable and relatively uniform' (adadi mutaqārib), meaning individual bricks do not vary significantly in value. Therefore, specifying the number and mold is sufficient to make the item known (ma‘lūm) and eliminates excessive uncertainty. This is consistent with the general principle that salam is valid in items whose description can be accurately captured.

Key Terms
لبن (labin): Unbaked brick (sun-dried).
آجر (ājur): Baked brick (kiln-fired).
ملبن (malban): The mold used to shape bricks; specifying it determines size and shape.

The General Rule for Salam: Specification of Description and Quantity
Establishing the universal condition for salam based on ability to describe and measure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid salam (ḍabṭ al-ṣifah wa al-miqtār) and apply it to determine whether salam is valid for a given commodity.

Core Idea
Salam is valid only if the item’s description and quantity can be precisely specified, avoiding dispute.

This passage from al-Quduri presents the foundational maxim for salam in Hanafi fiqh. The rule is simple but powerful: a salam contract is only permissible when the subject matter can be precisely described and quantified. Without this, the contract leads to uncertainty (gharar) and disputes, which Islamic law prohibits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the opening sentence: 'Every item whose description can be precisely specified and whose quantity can be known, salam in it is permissible.' This is the master condition.
The condition is two-fold: description (ṣifah) and quantity (miqdār).
2
Understand the rationale
The text explains why: 'because it does not lead to dispute (lā yufḍī ilā al-munāza‘ah).' The opposite case leads to unknown (majhūl) and dispute.
The legal reasoning (ta‘līl) is based on preventing harm (ḍarar) and uncertainty.
3
Apply the rule to examples
The author gives examples: basins (ṭast), bottles (qumquma), leather socks (khuffān) are valid if known; otherwise invalid. Map each example to the condition: can their description and quantity be specified?
The condition is met if the item is known (ma‘rūf) through description.
4
Recognize the nature of salam subject matter
The item in salam is a debt (dayn) on the seller until delivery. Without clear description, the debt is unknown (majhūl), which invalidates the contract.
This reinforces why precise specification is essential: the seller owes a determinate obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بیع سلم کا قاعدہ کلیہ
قال وكل ما امكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة مقداره جاز السلم فيه لانه لا يفضى الى المنازعة و ما لا يُضبط صفته و لا يعرف مقداره لا يجوز السلم فيه لانه دين و بدون الوصف يبقى مجهولا جهالة تفضى الى المنازعة ولا بأس بالسلم في طست او قمقمة او خفين او نحو ذلك اذا كان يعرف لاجتماع شرائط السلم وان كان لا يعرف فلا خير فيه لانه دين مجهول
ترجمہ اور ہر ایسی چیز جس کی صفت کو منضبط کرنا اور اس کی مقدار پہنچانا ممکن ہو تو اس میں مسلم جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہے۔ اور جس چیز کی صفت منضبط نہ ہو سکتی ہو اور نہ اس کی مقدار معلوم ہو سکتی ہو تو اس میں بیع سلم جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ مال دین ہے اور بغیر وصف کے ایسی مجہول رہے گی جو مفضی الی المنازعہ ہو۔ اور طشت یا قمہ یا چمڑے کے موزے یا اس کے مانند دوسری چیزوں مثلا کٹورہ ، گلاس ، لوہے اور پیتل کے برتنوں کی بیع سلم جائز ہے بشرطیکہ بیان اوصاف سے یہ چیزیں معلوم ہو جائیں کیونکہ اس صورت میں مسلم کی تمام شرطیں جمع ہو جاتی ہیں اور اگر کوئی چیز بیان اوصاف کے باوجود معلوم نہ ہو سکتی ہو تو
The General Rule for Salam
He said: And every item whose description can be precisely specified and whose quantity can be known, salam in it is permissible because it does not lead to dispute. And whatever item whose description cannot be precisely specified and whose quantity cannot be known, salam in it is not permissible because it is a debt (dayn) and without description it remains unknown (majhūl) in a way that leads to dispute. And there is no harm in salam in a basin (tast), a bottle (qumquma), leather socks (khuffān), or the like, if they are known, since the conditions of salam are met. And if they are not known, then there is no good in it because it is an unknown debt.
Translation: And every item whose description can be controlled and whose quantity can be known, salam in it is permissible because it does not lead to dispute. And whatever item whose description cannot be controlled and whose quantity cannot be known, salam in it is not permissible because it is a debt property and without description it remains unknown in a way that leads to dispute. And salam in a basin, bottle, leather socks, or similar items is permissible, provided they become known through description, because in that case all conditions of salam are met. And if an item cannot become known despite description, then there is no good in it because it is an unknown debt.
Explanation: (the Urdu commentary continues but is cut off).
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational maxim for salam in Hanafi fiqh. The commentator, al-Quduri, establishes a general rule: salam is valid in any commodity whose description (ṣifah) can be precisely stated and whose quantity (miqdār) can be determined. The rationale is that such specification prevents future dispute (tanāzu‘). Conversely, if either description or quantity cannot be fixed, salam is invalid because the subject matter remains unknown (majhūl) and leads to conflict. The rule is then applied to examples: basins, bottles, leather socks, etc., are valid if their descriptions make them known. This reinforces the core principle that uncertainty (gharar) is avoided in salam contracts, as the item is a debt (dayn) owed by the seller and must be clearly defined.

Key Terms
ضبط صفته (ḍabṭ ṣifatih): The ability to precisely specify and record the description of an item so that it is clearly defined.
مقدار (miqdār): The quantity or measure of the item, which must be known for salam to be valid.
دين (dayn): A debt or obligation that is owed; in salam, the subject matter is a dayn on the seller until delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the buyer must see the item

Key Takeaway
Salam is valid only when the subject matter's description and quantity are precisely specifiable, preventing disputes arising from uncertainty.

Salam in Bricks with Specified Mold
Ruling on salam in bricks conditioned on specifying the mold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on salam in bricks and explain why specifying the mold is necessary according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Salam in bricks is valid only when the mold is specified, because bricks are homogeneous countable items ('adadi mutaqarib).

In this passage from the chapter on Salam, the author addresses a practical transaction: paying in advance for bricks to be delivered later. The ruling clarifies that such a contract is permissible, but with a key condition. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the broader Hanafi principle that salam is allowed for items that are uniform and can be described with precision, avoiding uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a salam contract for bricks. The ruling is that it is permissible (jā'iz) when the mold is specified.
2
Spot the condition
The passage states 'idha samma mlibanan ma'luman' (when a known mold is specified). This is the key condition for validity.
3
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The reason given is that bricks are 'adadi mutaqarib (homogeneous countable items), meaning each unit is similar in value, minimizing uncertainty.
4
Appreciate the emphasis on specification
The commentary adds 'la siyyama idha' (especially when) the mold is specified, indicating that specification removes remaining ambiguity.
5
Consider cross-madhhab implications
Other schools may require more detailed descriptions, but the Hanafi view relies on the nature of the item being homogeneous countable.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

The General Rule for Salam: Valid When Description and Quantity are Specifiable
General rule for salam with rational proof and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for salam contracts and apply it to determine the validity of salam in different scenarios.

Core Idea
Salam is valid only if the subject matter can be precisely described and quantified to avoid dispute.

This passage from al-Hidayah lays down the foundational principle for salam contracts. You will learn that the key to validity is the ability to define the item’s description (ḍabṭ ṣifah) and quantity (miqdār). The passage provides both a rational proof and concrete examples to guide the application of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the opening statement: everything whose description can be defined and quantity known, salam is permissible; otherwise not.
This is a two-part condition: description and quantity.
2
Extract the illa (underlying reason)
The author states the reason: such items do not lead to dispute (lā yufḍī ilā al-munāza‘ah). Conversely, unspecified items lead to dispute.
This is a mafsadah-based reasoning.
3
Examine the proof
The proof is based on the nature of salam: it creates a debt (dayn). An unspecified debt is majhūl and leads to dispute, which invalidates the contract.
This is a rational proof (talīl), not a direct nass.
4
Apply the rule to examples
Consider the examples (basin, flagon, leather socks). They are permissible if their description renders them known; otherwise not.
The examples show that manufactured items can also be subject to salam if specifiable.
5
Recognize the scope
The rule applies universally to all items, not just agricultural goods. Only the ability to describe and quantify matters.
This is a general principle in Hanafi fiqh.

بیع سلم کا قاعدہ کلیہ
قال وكل ما امكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة مقداره جاز السلم فيه لانه لا يفضى الى المنازعة و ما لا يُضبط صفته و لا يعرف مقداره لا يجوز السلم فيه لانه دين و بدون الوصف يبقى مجهولا جهالة تفضى الى المنازعة ولا بأس بالسلم في طست او قمقمة او خفين او نحو ذلك اذا كان يعرف لاجتماع شرائط السلم وان كان لا يعرف فلا خير فيه لانه دين مجهول
ترجمہ اور ہر ایسی چیز جس کی صفت کو منضبط کرنا اور اس کی مقدار پہنچانا ممکن ہو تو اس میں مسلم جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہے۔ اور جس چیز کی صفت منضبط نہ ہو سکتی ہو اور نہ اس کی مقدار معلوم ہو سکتی ہو تو اس میں بیع سلم جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ مال دین ہے اور بغیر وصف کے ایسی مجہول رہے گی جو مفضی الی المنازعہ ہو۔ اور طشت یا قمہ یا چمڑے کے موزے یا اس کے مانند چیزوں میں مسلم کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے بشرطیکہ وہ معلوم ہو کیونکہ مسلم کی شرطیں جمع ہیں اور اگر معلوم نہ ہو تو اس کی سلم میں خیر نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مجہول دین ہے۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری اس عبارت میں ایک ضابطہ بیان فرماتے ہیں۔ اس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جس چیز کی صفت کو منضبط کرنا اور اس کی مقدار کو پہچانناممکن ہو اس کی بیع سلم جائز ہے جیسے مکیلات موزونات ، مزروعات اور عد دیات متقاربہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ایسی چیز کی سپردگی میں کسی جھگڑے کا امکان نہیں ہے اور جب جھگڑے کا امکان نہیں تو بیع سلم بھی جائز ہوگی۔ اور جس چیز کی صفت کا منضبط کرنا اور اس کی مقدار کا پہچانا ممکن نہ ہو تو اس کی بیع سلم جائز نہیں ہے۔ اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مسلم فیہ دین ہوتی ہے یعنی مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ واجب ہوتی ہے اور دین یعنی جو چیز مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ میں واجب ہے وہ بغیر بیان وصف کے مجہول اور غیر معلوم رہے گی اور مجہول بھی ایسی جو مفضی الی المنازعہ ہوگی اور ایسی جہالت جو انجام کار کے اعتبار سے جھگڑا پیدا کرے، مفسد بیع ہوتی ہے اس لیے اگر مسلم فیہ کا وصف منضبط نہ ہو اور اس کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہو تو اس کی بیع سلم جائز نہ ہوگی۔ صاحب قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ طشت ، قمقمہ، چمڑے کے موزے اور ان کے مانند دوسری چیزوں مثلا کٹورہ ، گلاس ، لوہے اور پیتل کے برتنوں کی بیع سلم جائز ہے بشرطیکہ بیان اوصاف سے یہ چیزیں معلوم ہو جائیں کیونکہ اس صورت میں مسلم کی تمام شرطیں جمع ہو جاتی ہیں اور اگر کوئی چیز بیان اوصاف کے باوجود معلوم نہ ہو سکتی ہو تو اس کی بیع سلم جائز نہ ہوگی۔
General rule of salam: He said: And everything whose description can be defined and whose quantity can be known, salam in it is permissible because it does not lead to dispute. And what cannot have its description defined nor its quantity known, salam in it is not permissible because it is a debt, and without description it remains unknown with ignorance that leads to dispute. And there is no harm in salam in a basin, a flagon, leather socks, or the like when they are known, because the conditions of salam are met; if they are not known, there is no good in it because it is an unknown debt. Translation: And every item whose description can be defined and whose quantity can be determined, salam in it is permissible because it does not lead to dispute. And an item whose description cannot be defined nor its quantity known, salam in it is not permissible because it is a debt and without description it remains unknown with ignorance that leads to dispute. And there is no harm in salam in a basin, flagon, leather socks, or similar items provided they are known because the conditions of salam are met; if not known, there is no good in it because it is an unknown debt. Explanation: The author of al-Quduri states a general rule: anything whose description can be defined and quantity known, salam is permissible, such as items sold by measure, weight, crops, and homogeneous countable items. The proof is that such items do not lead to dispute. And items whose description cannot be defined nor quantity known, salam is not permissible because the subject matter is a debt, and without specification it remains unknown, leading to dispute, which invalidates the sale. He says that basins, flagons, leather socks, and similar items like bowls, glasses, iron and brass vessels are permissible for salam if they can be known by description, because all conditions of salam are met; if not, it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational principle for salam contracts: validity depends on the ability to precisely describe and quantify the subject matter. The reasoning (illa) is that uncertainty (jahalah) leads to dispute, which is prohibited. Therefore, items like those sold by measure, weight, or homogeneous countable items are permissible. Examples include basins, flagons, and leather socks if their description renders them known. The commentary by the author explains that this rule is from al-Quduri's matn and emphasizes that if an item remains unknown even after description, salam is invalid. The Hanafi school applies this principle broadly to allow salam in many manufactured items as long as specification is possible.

Key Terms
ضبط صفته (dabt sifatihi): The ability to precisely define the characteristics (description) of the item.
مقدار (miqdar): Quantity, whether by measure, weight, count, etc.
دین (dayn): A debt obligation that is owed in the future.
جهالة مفضیة الی المنازعة (jahālah mufḍiyah ilā al-munāza‘ah): Ignorance that leads to dispute; such jahalah invalidates a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying reason (illa) for allowing salam in items whose description and quantity can be specified?
A) The price is paid in advance.
B) It avoids dispute.
C) The seller benefits.
D) It is a form of charity.

Key Takeaway
Salam is valid only when the subject matter is precisely defined in description and quantity to eliminate the risk of dispute.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Istisna': Validity by Istihsan Amidst Qiyas Objection
Ruling on istisna': valid by istihsan due to practical consensus, invalid by qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna', explain the basis of istihsan over qiyas, and distinguish istisna' from salam.

Core Idea
Istisna' is a valid sale by istihsan: continuous custom overrides the general prohibition of selling non-existent goods.

Istisna' (commission to manufacture) is a contract people use daily—ordering a custom-made couch or a specialized machine. Yet a strict analogy would forbid it as selling something that does not yet exist. The Hanafi school rules it valid by istihsan (juristic preference), relying on the continuous practice of Muslims since the Prophet's time. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi reasoning, the qiyas objection from Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and the practical implications of the contract's binding nature.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Istisna' without a fixed term is valid by istihsan as a sale, not a promise.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i hold it invalid by qiyas because it is a sale of a non-existent thing and does not meet salam conditions.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They cite the hadith 'Do not sell what you do not possess' and the necessity of a fixed term for salam.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Istihsan is based on practical consensus (ijma' 'amali) and the Prophet's own commissioning of a ring and pulpit. The non-existent is considered existent by legal fiction (hukman). The object of sale is the specific item, not the work.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In the Hanafi school, istisna' is a valid binding sale (with option for both parties until acceptance). In the Shafi'i school, it is invalid unless restructured as salam with a fixed term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى استصناع کا حکم
قال وان استصنع شيئا من ذلك بغير اجل جاز استحسانا للاجماع الثابت بالتعامل وفي القياس لا يجوز لانه بيع المعدوم والصحيح انه يجوز بيعا لا عدة والمعدوم قد يعتبر موجوداً حكمًا والمعقود عليه العين دون العمل حتى لو جاء به مفروعًا عنه لا من صنعته او من صنعته قبل العقد فاخذه جاز ولا يتعين الا بالاختيار ى لـو بـاعـ صانع قبل ان يراه المستصنع وهذا كــلـــه هـو الـصـحـيـح
ترجمہ اور اگر مذکورہ چیزوں میں سے کوئی چیز بغیر میعاد کے بنوائی تو استحسانا جائز ہے اس اجماع کی وجہ سے جو تعامل ناس سے ثابت ہے اور قیاس کے مطابق جائز نہیں ہے اس لیے کہ یہ معدوم کی بیع ہے اور صحیح یہ ہے کہ استصناع بطور بیع کے جائز ہے نہ کہ بطور وعدہ کے ۔ اور معدوم کو حکماً موجود شمار کیا جائیگا اور معقود علیہ عین شئی ہے نہ کہ عمل حتی کہ اگر کاریگر ایسی بنی ہوئی چیز کولا یا جو اس کی بنائی ہوئی نہیں ہے یا عقد سے پہلے کی بنائی ہوئی ہے پس بنوانے والے نے اس کو لے لیا تو جائز ہے اور وہ چیز متعین نہ ہو گی مگر بنوانے والے کے پسند کرنے سے حتی کہ اگر کار دیگر نے بنوانے والے کو دکھلانے سے پہلے اس کو بیچ دیا تو جائز ہے اور یہ سب تفصیل صحیح ہے۔ تشریح استصناع کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی کسی کام کے کاریگر سے کہے فلاں چیز ان ان اوصاف کے ساتھ اتنی لمبی چوڑی اتنے روپوں میں بنادے اور پوری رقم یا کچھ رقم کاریگر کو دیدے اور کوئی میعاد ذکر نہ کرے ہمارے یہاں اسی کو سائی دیکر بنوانا کہتے ہیں تو یہ استصناع استحسانا جائز ہے مگر قیا سا جائز نہیں ہے۔ وجہ استحسان عملی اجماع ہے کیونکہ عہد رسالت سے لے کر آج تک بلانکیر لوگوں کا اس پر تعامل چلا آرہا ہے اور لوگوں کا اجماع ایک محبت شرعیہ ہے جیسا کہ رسول خدا صلى الله عليه وسلم کا قول لا تجمتع امتى على ضلالة اور مار آہ المسلمون حسنا فهو عند اللہ حسن کے الفاظ حدیث اس پر کھلی شہادت میں اور اس سے بڑھ کر یہ ہے کہ خود رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے ایک انگوٹھی اور منبر سائی دیکر بنوائے ہیں پس رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم کے انگوٹھی اور منبر سائی دیگر بنوانے کے بعد استصناع کے جواز میں کیا شبہ رہ جاتا ہے اور وجہ قیاس جو امام زفر اور امام شافعی کا مذہب بھی یہ ہے کہ استصناع کی صورت میں جو چیز فروخت کی جاتی ہے وہ معدوم ہے اور معدوم کی بیع نا جائز ہے جیسا حديث نهى عن بيع ما ليس عند الانسان و رخص في السلم سے ظاہر ہوتا ہے یعنی معدوم کی بیع سے اللہ کے رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم نے منع فرمایا ہے البتہ سلم کی اجازت دی ہے مگر استصناع مسلم نہیں ہے کیونکہ سلم کے لئے اجل اور میعاد ضروری ہے اور استصناع کے لئے کوئی نہیں ہوتی پس جب استصناع بیع سلم نہیں ہے اور معدوم کی بیچ سے منع کیا گیا ہے تو استصناع نا جائز ہوگا۔ اب سوال یہ ہے کہ استصناع جائز ہونے کی صورت میں استصناع آیا بیچ ہے یا محض وعدہ ہے تو اس بارے میں صحیح قول یہ ہے کہ استصناع بیج ہے یہی عامتہ المشائخ کا مذہب ہے اور بعض حضرات کہتے ہیں کہ استصناع محض ایک وعدہ ہے عقد اس وقت منعقد ہو گا جبکہ شی تیار ہونے پر باہمی لین دین ہوگا یہی وجہ ہے کہ کاریگر اور بنوانے والے دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لیے خیار ثابت ہے یعنی کا ریگر کو نہ بنوانے کا اختیار ہے چنانچہ کاریگر اگر نہ بنانا چاہے تو اس کو مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا اور بنوانے والے کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ کاریگر جو چیز بنا کر لایا ہے
And heading: Ruling on istisna'. He said: And if one commissions something from that without a fixed term, it is permissible by istihsan due to the consensus established by practice, and by qiyas it is not permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent thing. The correct view is that it is permissible as a sale, not a promise. And the non-existent is considered existent by legal fiction, and the subject matter is the actual object (ayn) not the work. So if the craftsman brings it already made, not of his own making, or made before the contract, and the commissioner takes it, it is permissible. And it is not determined except by choice. So if the craftsman sells it before the commissioner sees it, it is permissible. All this is the correct view. Translation: And if one commissions something from among the mentioned items without a fixed term, it is permissible by istihsan due to the consensus established by mutual practice, and by qiyas it is not permissible because it is a sale of a non-existent thing. The correct view is that istisna' is valid as a sale, not a promise. And the non-existent is considered existent by legal fiction, and the subject matter is the actual object, not the work. So if the craftsman brings it already made, either not of his making or made before the contract, and the commissioner takes it, it is permissible. And it is not determined except by the commissioner's choice. So if the craftsman sells it before the commissioner sees it, it is permissible. All these details are correct. Explanation: The scenario of istisna' is that a person says to a craftsman: 'Make such and such item with these descriptions, so long and wide, for such and such rupees,' and gives the full or partial payment without specifying a term. This is called 'sai' (commission) in our usage. Istisna' is valid by istihsan but not by qiyas. The basis of istihsan is practical consensus (ijma' 'amali), because from the time of the Prophet to today, people have practiced it without objection. Consensus is a valid proof, as the hadith says: 'My community will not agree upon an error' and 'What Muslims see as good is good before Allah.' Moreover, the Prophet himself commissioned a ring and a pulpit. After this, there is no doubt about the permissibility of istisna'. As for qiyas, which is the view of Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, it holds that istisna' is a sale of a non-existent thing, and such sale is prohibited by the hadith: 'He forbade the sale of what one does not possess' and permitted only salam. But istisna' is not salam because salam requires a fixed term, while istisna' has none. So since it is not salam and the sale of non-existent is prohibited, istisna' is invalid. Now the question: Istisna'—is it a sale or merely a promise? The correct view is that it is a sale, which is the opinion of the majority of scholars. Some say it is only a promise, and the contract concludes when the item is ready and exchange occurs; hence both parties have an option: the craftsman may choose not to make it and cannot be compelled, and the commissioner may choose whether to accept the finished item.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage deals with istisna' (commission to manufacture) as a distinct contract from salam. The Hanafi school rules it valid by istihsan (juristic preference) based on continuous practice (ta'amul) since the Prophet's time, including the Prophet's own commissioning of a ring and a pulpit. The opposing view (Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i) argues by qiyas that it is a sale of a non-existent thing, which is prohibited; the Hanafi response distinguishes istisna' from an invalid sale of non-existent because istisna' has become accepted by customary practice. The passage further clarifies that istisna' is a binding sale, not a mere promise, although both parties retain an option (khiyar) to withdraw until acceptance. The subject matter is the manufactured object itself, not the work, allowing the craftsman to supply an already-made item.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisnā‘): A contract of commission to manufacture a specific item with given specifications, without a fixed term.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that departs from strict qiyas for a stronger reason (e.g., custom or necessity).
معدوم (ma‘dūm): Non-existent; something that does not exist at the time of contract.
تعامل (ta‘āmul): Mutual practice; customary usage among people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi permissibility of istisna'?
A) Qiyas on salam
B) Istihsan due to practical consensus
C) A direct Quranic verse
D) Mutawatir hadith

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a valid contract by istihsan, overriding the qiyas prohibition, because of continuous customary practice and the Prophet's own example.

Istisna' Without a Term: Permissibility by Istihsan
Ruling on istisna' contract without fixed term, using istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna' contracts without a fixed term and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas using the evidence of consensus.

Core Idea
Istisna' is valid because custom and consensus override the qiyas prohibition of selling non-existent goods.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling that highlights the role of istihsan (juristic preference) in overriding strict analogical reasoning. The contract of istisna' (manufacturing order) is a common transaction: you order a custom item from a craftsman. The question is whether it is valid when no delivery term is fixed. The Hanafi school, drawing on the continuous practice of the Muslim community since the Prophet's time, affirms its validity even though the item does not yet exist at contract formation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a manufacturing contract (istisna') without a fixed term. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permitted (ja'iz) by istihsan.
2
Contrast with qiyas
By qiyas (analogy to regular sale of non-existent goods), the contract would be invalid. Understand why qiyas would prohibit it: the subject matter is non-existent at contract time.
3
Examine the dalil: Ijma based on continuous practice
The proof for istihsan is consensus (ijma) established by the continuous practice (ta'amul) of Muslims since the time of the Prophet (ﷺ), including the Prophet himself ordering a ring and pulpit.
This practice constitutes a customary consensus that overrides the qiyas objection.
4
Understand the legal fiction
The passage states the non-existent may be considered existent as a legal fiction (hukman) for this contract. This is a key Hanafi mechanism to facilitate transactions.
5
Note the intra-madhhab and cross-madhhab dispute
Imam Zufar (within Hanafi) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold the contract invalid by qiyas. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is a valid, binding sale.
This illustrates how later tarjih favored istihsan over qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان استصنع شيئا من ذلك بغير اجل جاز استحسانا للاجماع الثابت بالتعامل وفي القياس لا يجوز لانه بيع المعدوم والصحيح انه يجوز بيعا لا عدة والمعدوم قد يعتبر موجوداً حكمًا
And if one orders something of that [type] without a term, it is permitted by istihsan due to the consensus established by [continuous] practice; and by qiyas it is not permitted because it is a sale of a non-existent thing. And the correct [view] is that it is a sale, not a promise, and the non-existent may be considered existent as a legal fiction.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage states the primary ruling of istisna' (contract of manufacture). The ruling: if a person orders a specific item from a craftsman without fixing a time for delivery, the contract is valid by istihsan (juristic preference). The proof is the consensus (ijma') established by the continuous practice of Muslims since the time of the Prophet (ﷺ). The Prophet himself ordered a ring and a pulpit to be made, which is explicit evidence. By strict qiyas, however, it would be invalid because the subject matter is non-existent at the time of contract (sale of ma'dum). The correct position, based on istihsan, is that the contract is a sale, not a mere promise. The non-existent is regarded as existent by legal fiction for the sake of facilitating transactions. This follows the Hanafi principle that istihsan can override qiyas when there is a stronger evidence or custom.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisnāʿ): A contract where a person orders a craftsman to manufacture a specific item with specified attributes, often with advance payment and without a fixed term.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling supported by stronger evidence or custom.
معدوم (maʿdūm): A non-existent object, one not yet in existence at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is istisna' (manufacturing contract) considered valid despite being a sale of a non-existent object?
A) Because it is a promise, not a sale.
B) Because of consensus (ijma') established by continuous practice, which overrides qiyas.
C) Because the object is deemed existent by shari'ah.
D) Because qiyas also permits it.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' without a fixed term is a valid, binding sale by istihsan, because the continuous practice of Muslims constitutes consensus that overrides the qiyas prohibition of selling non-existent goods.

Subject Matter of Istisna' is the Manufactured Item, Not the Work
Clarification that the contract is on the finished good, not the labour

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the subject matter of istisna' is the manufactured item (al-'ayn) and explain the consequences for the craftsman's obligation and the customer's right of choice.

Core Idea
In istisna', the contract is on the good itself, not the work — so the craftsman may deliver any compliant item, even without personal manufacture.

This passage clarifies a foundational principle of istisna': the subject matter is the manufactured item, not the craftsman's labour. Understanding this distinction determines several practical rulings: the craftsman is not obliged to personally make the item, and the customer retains a right of choice (khiyar al-ru'ya) upon delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
Read the first part of the passage: 'And the subject of the contract is the [finished] good itself, not the work.' This tells us the contract is a sale of a good, not a hire of services.
2
Derive the craftsman's obligation
Since the contract is on the good, the craftsman is not required to personally manufacture it. He may deliver a ready-made item from another source or a pre-made item.
3
Identify the customer's right of choice
The passage says 'it is not determined except by choice.' The customer has khiyar al-ru'ya: he can accept or reject after seeing the item.
Choice is exercised at delivery.
4
Apply to third-party sale
If the craftsman sells the item to another before showing it to the first customer, the sale is valid because the item was not yet determined for the first customer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعقود عليه العين دون العمل حتى لو جاء به مفروعًا عنه لا من صنعته او من صنعته قبل العقد فاخذه جاز ولا يتعين الا بالاختيار لو باعه صانع قبل ان يراه المستصنع وهذا كله هو الصحيح
And the subject of the contract is the [finished] good itself, not the work. So even if he brings it already finished, not from his own craftsmanship, or from his craftsmanship before the contract, and [the customer] takes it, it is permissible. And it is not determined except by choice, so if the craftsman sells it before the customer sees it, it is permissible. And all of this is the correct [view].
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage clarifies what is the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh) in an istisna' contract. The correct view is that the contract is on the actual manufactured item (al-'ayn), not on the craftsman's work or service ('amal). The implication is that the craftsman is not required to personally manufacture the item; he may deliver a ready-made item that he obtained from elsewhere or one that he made before the contract. The customer has the right of choice (khiyar al-ru'ya) upon seeing the item, so the item is not finally sold until the customer approves it. Therefore, if the craftsman sells the item to a third party before showing it to the customer, the sale is valid because the item was not yet determined as sold to the first customer. This distinction is necessary to maintain the flexibility of istisna' as a sale and not a mere promise.

Key Terms
معقود عليه (maʿqūd ʿalayh): The subject matter of the contract; in istisna', it is the manufactured good itself, not the labour.
عين (ʿayn): A tangible, specific object; the opposite of 'amal (work).
اختيار (ikhtiyār): The right of choice (khiyar al-ru'ya) that the customer has upon seeing the delivered item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the work is part of the contract.
B) Yes, because the contract is on the good, not the specific craftsmanship.
C) Only if the customer agreed beforehand.
D) Only if the craftsman pays compensation.

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of istisna' is the manufactured good itself, so the craftsman is not bound to personally make it, and the customer has the right to inspect and reject upon delivery.

Option (Khiyar) in Istisna': Customer and Artisan
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya for customer and dispute on artisan's khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar in istisna' for both parties and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
In istisna‘, the customer has the option to reject upon sight; the artisan does not — the correct view prevents contractual instability.

The contract of istisna' (manufacturing) allows a customer to order a custom item unseen. Naturally, upon delivery, the customer needs a right to reject if the item does not meet expectations. But does the artisan also have a right to cancel? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on khiyar in istisna', focusing on who holds the option and why the majority denies it to the artisan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their options
First, note that the passage discusses two parties: the customer (mustasni') and the artisan (sani'). Identify what each is entitled to according to the text.
The customer has khiyar al-ru'ya; the artisan does not according to the more correct view.
2
Locate the basis for the customer's option
The passage states 'because he purchased something he has not seen.' This is the key proof: the general principle that a buyer who contracts for an unseen object has the right to reject upon seeing it.
This is consistent with the hadith: 'من اشترى شيئا لم يره فهو بالخيار إذا رآه' (Whoever buys something unseen has the option when he sees it).
3
Note the dispute on the artisan's option
The text mentions a narration from Abu Hanifa that the artisan also has an option. Understand the opposing proof: the artisan cannot deliver without the customer's acceptance, causing hardship.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the majority (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and later scholars) reject this view.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preponderance)
The text declares the view that the artisan has no option as 'al-ashah' (more correct). Identify the reason: the artisan can sell the item to another buyer, alleviating the claimed hardship.
This tarjih is from al-Mabsut and al-Hidayah, making it the mufta bihi.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of the dispute: if the artisan had an option, he could rescind the contract even after the customer accepts, creating instability. The correct ruling prevents this.
Thus, the customer's acceptance binds the contract; the artisan cannot back out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال وهو بالخيار ان شاء اخذه وان شاء تركه لانه اشترى شيئا لم يره ولا خيار للصانع كذا ذكره في المبسوط وهو الاصح لأنه باع مالم يره وعن ابي حنيفة ان له الخيار ايضا لانه لا يمكنه تسليم المعقود عليه
And he said: And he has the option: if he wishes he may take it, and if he wishes he may leave it, because he purchased something he has not seen. And there is no option for the craftsman. Thus it is mentioned in al-Mabsut, and it is the more correct [view] because he sold what he has not seen. And from Abu Hanifa that he also has an option because he cannot deliver the subject matter [without the customer's acceptance].
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage from the matn of al-Hidayah discusses the right of option (khiyar) in istisna'. The ruling: the customer (mustasni') has the khiyar al-ru'ya (option upon sight) — he may accept or reject the delivered item. This is because he contracted for an unseen object. As for the craftsman (sani'), the correct and relied-upon position (as in al-Mabsut) is that he has no such option, because he also sold what he had not seen (the customer's acceptance). However, there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that the artisan does have an option, due to the difficulty of delivering the subject matter if the customer refuses. The majority prefers the first view, as the artisan can always sell the item to someone else. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the mufta bihi is that the artisan has no khiyar.

Key Terms
خيار الروية (khiyār al-ruʾya): The option to accept or reject a purchased item upon seeing it for the first time after contract.
صانع (ṣāniʿ): The craftsman or seller in an istisna' contract; the party who manufactures or supplies the item.
مستصنع (mustaṣniʿ): The customer who orders the manufacture of an item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position, who has khiyar (option) in an istisna' contract?
A) Both the customer and the artisan.
B) Only the customer.
C) Only the artisan.
D) Neither party.

Key Takeaway
The customer has khiyar al-ru'ya in istisna'; the artisan does not, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Istisna' as a Valid Sale Despite Non-Existent Subject Matter
Establishes Istisna' as a sale by refuting the objection of non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school treats Istisna' as a valid sale despite the non-existence of the subject matter at contract formation.

Core Idea
Istisna' is a sale because Shari'ah and custom treat the non-existent as existent when needed.

This lesson examines a key objection to Istisna' (manufacturing contract): that the subject matter does not exist at the time of contract, seemingly making it an invalid sale. The Hanafi school, relying on Imam Muhammad's position in al-Mabsut, refutes this by showing that Shari'ah itself recognizes non-existent objects as existent in certain circumstances, such as forgotten tasmiyah and istihadah purity. Custom (ta'amul) further supports this treatment, making Istisna' a valid sale with khiyar al-ru'yat and qiyas/istihsan applicable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central objection
The objection is that selling a non-existent object (ma'dum) is invalid. Istisna' involves a future manufactured item, so it cannot be a sale.
2
State the Hanafi proof that it is a sale
Imam Muhammad in al-Mabsut calls Istisna' a sale, applies khiyar al-ru'yat (option of inspection) and qiyas/istihsan, which are features of sales, not promises.
This shows the ruling from a primary source.
3
Understand the response to the objection
The author cites the principle that sometimes non-existent is considered existent by Shari'ah, giving examples: the forgetful slaughterer's tasmiyah and the istihadah woman's purity.
Both examples involve excusable circumstances where Shari'ah overlooks non-existence.
4
Apply the analogy to Istisna'
Similarly, due to the widespread custom (ta'amul) of people engaging in manufacturing contracts, the non-existent item is treated as existent, validating the sale.
Custom (urf) is a recognized source in Hanafi mu'amalat.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, Istisna' is a valid sale (bay'), not a mere promise (wa'd), and the objection based on non-existence is removed.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وہ اس کو قبول نہ کرے اس کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس طرح کا اختیار وعدہ کے اندر تو ہوتا ہے بیع کے اندر نہیں ہوتا۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ استصناع محض وعدہ سے اور بیع نہیں۔ عامة المشائخ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ امام محمد نے استصناع کو مبسوط میں بیع کہا ہے۔ اور اس میں خیار رویت ثابت کیا ہے اور اس میں قیاس اور استحسان کو جاری کیا ہے اور خیار رؤیت اور قیاس و استحسان بیع میں جاری ہوتے ہیں وعدے میں جاری نہیں ہوتے اس لئے استصناع بیع ہو گا وعدہ نہ ہوگا۔ والمعدوم قد يعتبر موجوداً الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ استصناع، بیع کیسے ہو سکتا ہے درآنحالیکہ معدوم بیع بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا ؟ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ کبھی کبھی بحکم شرع معدوم کو موجود شمار کر لیا جاتا ہے جیسے ذبح کے وقت بھول کر تسمیہ ترک کرنے والے کا تسمیہ معدوم ہے مگر عذر نسیان کی وجہ سے موجود شمار کیا گیا ہے اور متروک التسمیہ ناسیاً کے کھانے کی اجازت دی گئی ہے۔ اور جیسے مستحاضہ کی طہارت معدوم ہے لیکن جواز صلوۃ کے عذر کی وجہ سے اس کو موجود شمار کیا گیا ہے اسی طرح جس چیز کو کاریگر بنائے گا بلا شبہ وہ معدوم ہے لیکن تعامل ناس کی وجہ سے اس کو موجود شمار کر لیا گیا اور جب استصناع میں بیع معدوم کو موجود شمار کر لیا گیا تو بیع کے معدوم ہونے کا اعتراض واقع نہ ہوگا۔
He [the artisan] does not accept it; he will not be forced to accept it. And this type of option exists in promise, not in sale. So it became clear that Istisna' is not a mere promise but a sale. The proof of the general scholars is that Imam Muhammad called Istisna' a sale in al-Mabsut. And he established khiyar al-ru'yat (option of inspection) in it, and he applied qiyas and istihsan in it. And khiyar al-ru'yat and qiyas and istihsan apply in sale, not in promise; therefore Istisna' is a sale, not a promise. And 'al-ma'dum qad yu'tabaru mawjudan' (the non-existent may be considered existent) etc. is an answer to a question. The question is: how can Istisna' be a sale when the non-existent does not have the capacity to be sold? The answer: sometimes by the ruling of shari'ah the non-existent is considered existent, like the one who forgets and leaves the tasmiyah at the time of slaughter; his tasmiyah is non-existent but due to the excuse of forgetfulness it is considered existent, and eating of the one who omitted tasmiyah forgetfully is permitted. And like the istihadah woman's purity is non-existent but due to the excuse of permitting prayer it is considered existent. Similarly, the thing that the artisan will make is undoubtedly non-existent, but due to the custom of the people it is considered existent. And when the sale of non-existent is considered existent in Istisna', the objection of the sale being non-existent will not arise.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing that Istisna' is a valid sale in the Hanafi school, not merely a promise. It addresses an objection regarding selling a non-existent object. The author states that the general mashayikh rely on Imam Muhammad's view in al-Mabsut, where he treats Istisna' as a sale by applying khiyar al-ru'yat and qiyas/istihsan, which are only applicable to sales. The response to the objection is that shari'ah sometimes considers non-existent as existent under necessity or custom, analogizing it to the cases of forgetting tasmiyah in slaughter (considered existent due to forgetfulness) and the ritual purity of a woman with chronic bleeding (considered existent due to necessity). Similarly, custom (ta'amul) makes the non-existent manufactured item valid for sale. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah as per Imam Muhammad's position.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisnāʿ): A contract for commissioning the manufacture of a specific item, treated as a sale in the Hanafi school.
وعد (waʿd): A promise, which is not legally binding in the sense of a contract.
معدوم (maʿdūm): Something that does not exist at the time of contract.
تعامل (taʿāmul): Customary practice of people in transactions, used as a basis for istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is actually a promise, not a sale.
B) The non-existent is considered existent due to custom (ta'amul) and shari'ah recognition.
C) The artisan's work is considered the subject matter.
D) The object becomes existent at the time of contract by legal fiction.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a valid sale in the Hanafi school because Shari'ah and custom treat the non-existent as existent under necessity.

Subject Matter of Istisna' is the Manufactured Item, Not the Work
Defines the ma'qud alayh of Istisna' as the item, not the labor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the subject matter of Istisna' and explain how it is determined, and identify the legal consequences of this definition.

Core Idea
The subject matter of Istisna' is the manufactured item, not the artisan's work.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a fundamental question: what exactly is being contracted in an Istisna' (manufacturing) contract? Is it the artisan's labor or the final item itself? The Hanafi position is decisive and has important practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the core question is what constitutes the ma'qud alayh (subject matter) in an Istisna' contract.
The passage begins by stating 'the subject matter is the item'.
2
State the Hanafi position
The ma'qud alayh is the manufactured item itself, not the work or labor of the artisan.
This is explicitly stated and is the foundation of the ruling.
3
Follow the proof
The author provides two scenarios: (1) the artisan brings an item made by another artisan, and (2) the artisan brings an item he made before the contract. Both are valid, proving that the subject matter is the item, not the work.
If the subject matter were the work, these scenarios would be invalid because the work is either not from this artisan or predates the contract.
4
Understand the implication
Because the subject matter is the item, the contract does not require the artisan personally to perform the work; the delivered item can be sourced elsewhere.
This gives flexibility in fulfillment.
5
Note the determination rule
The item becomes specifically determined (muta'ayyin) only when the customer approves it. Until then, the artisan may sell it to another.
This flows from the fact that the specific item is not identified until acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و المعقود عليه العين ... الخ سے صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ معقود علیہ یعنی میچ وہ چیز ہے جس کو کاریگر نے بنایا ہے کاریگر کا عمل معقود علیہ نہیں ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کاریگر ایسی چیز لے کر آیا جو خود اس کی بنائی ہوئی نہیں ہے بلکہ کسی دوسرے کا ریگر نے بنائی ہے یا اس چیز کو لایا جو اس نے عقد استصناع سے پہلے بنائی تھی اور بنوانے والے نے اس کو پسند کر کے لے لیا تو جائز ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ معقود علیہ کاریگر کا عمل نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ معین مصنوعہ ہے جس کو کاریگر نے عقد سے پہلے بنایا ہے یا کسی دوسرے کا ریگر سے بنوائی ہے۔ اگر معقود علیہ کا ریگر کا عمل ہوتا تو مذکورہ دونوں صورتوں میں استصناع درست نہ ہوتا۔ کیونکہ ایک صورت میں اس کاری گر کا عمل ہی نہیں پایا گیا بلکہ دوسرے کاری گر کا عمل پایا گیا اور ایک صورت میں اس کا عمل تو پایا گیا مگر عقد اس سے پہلے پایا گیا اور ظاہر ہے کہ عقد استصناع سے پہلے کا عمل معقود علیہ نہیں ہو سکتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس چیز کو بنوایا گیا ہے وہ اس وقت متعین ہوگی جبکہ بنوانے والا اس کو پسند کر لے حتی کہ اگر کاریگر نے بنوانے والے کو دکھانے سے پہلے اس کو بیچ دیا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ جب ابھی تک وہ چیز متعین نہیں ہوئی تو مبیع بھی نہ ہوگی۔ صاحب بدائع فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ جو کچھ مذکور ہوا یعنی استصناع وعدہ نہیں ہے بلکہ بیع ہے اور یہ بیع جس چیز پر واقع ہوئی وہ کاریگر کا عمل نہیں بالکل بنائی ہوئی چیز ہے اور وہ بنوانے والے کے پسند کرنے سے پہلے متعین نہیں ہوئی یہ سب صحیح ہے۔
And 'the subject matter is the [manufactured] item...' etc. The author of Hidayah says: the subject matter—i.e., the match [thing]—is the thing that the artisan made; the work of the artisan is not the subject matter. For this reason, if the artisan brings something that he himself did not make but another artisan made, or if he brings something that he made before the Istisna' contract, and the customer accepts it and takes it, it is valid. From this it is known that the subject matter is not the work of the artisan but rather the specific manufactured item that the artisan made before the contract or had made by another artisan. If the subject matter were the artisan's work, then in both mentioned cases Istisna' would not be valid. Because in one case the work of this artisan is not found, but that of another artisan, and in the other case his work is found but before the contract, and it is obvious that work before the Istisna' contract cannot be the subject matter. The author of Hidayah says: the thing ordered becomes determined when the customer likes it; even if the artisan sold it before showing the customer, it is valid because when it is not yet determined, it is not the object of sale. The author of Bada'i' says: all this that has been mentioned—that Istisna' is not a promise but a sale, and the sale occurred on the thing, not the work, and it is not determined before the customer's approval—is correct.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling clarifying what constitutes the subject matter (ma'qud alayh) in Istisna'. It states that the subject matter is the manufactured item itself, not the artisan's labor. This is proven by two scenarios: if the artisan delivers an item made by another artisan, or one he made prior to the contract, the contract is still valid. This would not hold if the subject matter were the work, since in the first case the artisan performed no work, and in the second case the work predates the contract. The passage also notes that the item becomes specifically determined (ta'ayyun) only upon the customer's approval; until then, the artisan may sell it to another as it is not yet the specific object of sale. The author of al-Bada'i' confirms this view.

Key Terms
معقود علیہ (maʿqūd ʿalayh): The subject matter of a contract; in Istisna', it is the specific manufactured item.
متعین (mutaʿayyin): Specifically determined; the item becomes determined only after customer approval.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an artisan brings an item made by another artisan under an Istisna' contract, and the customer accepts it, is the contract valid?
A) No, because the artisan did not do the work himself.
B) Yes, because the subject matter is the item, not the work.
C) No, because the item was not made specifically for this customer.
D) Yes, only if the customer knew beforehand.

Key Takeaway
The ma'qud alayh in Istisna' is the manufactured item itself, not the artisan's work, allowing flexibility in sourcing and timing.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Khiyar in Istisna': Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Option of Inspection
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar in Istisna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on who has khiyar al-ru'yat in Istisna', identify the mufta bihi view, and explain the rationale behind each position.

Core Idea
In Istisna', the customer has khiyar al-ru'yat; the artisan does not—this is the preferred Hanafi view despite internal disagreement.

The contract of Istisna' (manufacturing) is a unique sale. When the customer orders an item unseen, when it is completed and he sees it, does he have the right to reject? What about the artisan? The Hanafi school has three different answers, each rooted in concern for fairness and the practical realities of custom. This lesson unpacks the dispute and shows why one view is chosen for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views: (1) Customer has option only (Imam Muhammad), (2) Both have option (Abu Hanifa), (3) Neither has option (Abu Yusuf).
These are all intra-Hanafi riwayat.
2
Follow the proof for each
For each position, identify the underlying rationale: the customer bought unseen, the artisan may suffer harm from cutting materials, the customer's option harms the artisan if the item is custom-made.
The third view balances harm to both parties.
3
Trace the authority hierarchy
Recognize that al-Mabsut (Imam Muhammad) is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is preferred. The other views are nawadir.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah vs. nawadir.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the customer rejects, the artisan may be stuck with the item under some views, but under the sahih view the artisan must accept the rejection.
This affects business risk.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
State that in fatwa, only the customer has khiyar al-ru'yat. The artisan has no option to cancel.
This is the operative ruling.
