غیبت معروفہ ہو تو اس کے دین میں نہ بیچا جائے
قال ومن اشترى عبدا فغاب والعبد فى يد البائع واقام البائع البينة انه باعه اياه فان كانت غيبته معروفة لم يُبع في دين البائع لانه يمكن ايصال البائع الى حقه بدون البيع وفيه ابطال حق المشترى وان لم يُدر اين هو بيع العبد وأوفى الثمن لان ملک المشترى ظهر باقراره فيظهر على الوجه الذى اقر به مشغولا بحقه واذا تعذر استيفاءه من المشترى يبيعه القاضى فيه كالراهن اذا مات والمشترى اذا مات مفلسا والمبيع لم يُقبض بخلاف ما بعد القبض لإن حقه لم يبق متعلقا به ثم ان فضل شيء يمسك للمشترى لانه بدل حقه وان نقص يتبع هو ايضا
ترجمہ اگر کوئی شخص غلام خرید کر غائب ہو گیا اور غلام بائع کے قبضہ میں ہے اور بائع نے بینہ پیش کیا کہ بائع نے یہ غلام فروخت کر دیا ہے پس اگر مشتری کا غائب ہونا معلوم ہوتو یہ غلام بائع کے قرضہ میں نہیں بیچا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ بائع کا اپنے حق تک پہنچنا بغیر فروخت کیئے
ممکن ہے اور فروخت کرنے میں مشتری کے حق کو باطل کرنا ہے اور اگر یہ معلوم نہیں کہ مشتری کہاں ہے تو غلام فروخت کیا جائے اور بائع کا شمن ادا کیا جائے اسلئے کہ مشتری کا مالک ہونا بائع کے اقرار سے ظاہر ہوا ہے تو جس طور پر اس نے اقرار کیا ہے یعنی اپنے حق کے ساتھ مشغول کر کے اسی طور پر ظاہر ہوگا اور جب مشتری سے بائع کے حق کا وصول ہونا مستعد ر ہو گیا تو قاضی غلام کو اس حق میں فروخت کر دے گا جیسے را ہن جب مر گیا ہو اور مشتری جب مفلس ہو کر مر گیا ہو۔ حالانکہ مبیع پر قبضہ نہیں کیا گیا ہے۔ برخلاف مابعد القبض کے۔ اسلئے کہ بائع کا حق مبیع کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں رہا پھر اگر کچھ بچ گیا ہو تو اس کو مشتری کیلئے روک لیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ مشتری کے حق کا عوض ہے۔ اور اگر کم پڑ گیا تو مشتری کا پیچھا کیا جائے گا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک غلام خریدا، پھر مشتری ہیچ یعنی غلام پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے غائب ہو گیا اور ابھی تک مشتری نے شمن بھی ادا نہیں کیا ہے۔ ایسی صورت میں اگر بائع ، قاضی سے یہ درخواست کرے کہ مذکورہ غلام بیچ کر میر امن ادا کیا جائے ، تو قاضی بائع کے دعوی کی طرف متوجہ نہیں ہوگا۔ ہاں اگر بائع نے اس بات پر گواہ پیش کر دیئے کہ میں نے فلاں آدمی جو غائب ہے اس کو اپنا غلام فروخت کیا تھا مگر اس نے نہ ثمن ادا کیا اور نہ ہی پر قبضہ کیا تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں مشتری کی غیبت معلوم ہوگی یا معلوم نہ ہوگی۔ یعنی یا تو یہ معلوم ہو گا کہ مشتری کہاں ہے اور یا یہ معلوم نہ ہو گا۔ اگر مشتری کا ٹھکانا معلوم ہے تو بائع کا دین ادا کرنے کیلئے اس غلام کو فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ اس صورت میں بغیر غلام فروخت کئے بائع اپنا حق وصول کر سکتا ہے بایں طور کہ مشتری جہاں موجود ہے وہاں جا کر اس سے اپنا ثمن وصول کرلے اور غلام بیچنے میں مشتری کا حق باطل ہو جائے گا اور کسی کے حق کو باطل کرنا بھی درست نہیں ہے پس جب غلام فروخت کرنے میں مشتری کے حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے اور بغیر فروخت کیئے بائع اپنا حق وصول کر سکتا ہے تو بائع کا دین شمن ادا کرنے کیلئے غلام کو فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا اور اگر مشتری کا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہو کہ کہاں ہے تو ایسی صورت میں مذکورہ غلام فروخت کر کے بائع کا شمن ادا کر دیا جائے۔ کیونکہ بائع کے اقرار سے تو ظاہر ہو گیا کہ مشتری اس غلام کا مالک ہے۔ پس جس طور پر اس نے اقرار کیا ہے۔ اسی طور پر مشتری کی ملک ظاہر ہوگی۔ اور بائع کا اقرار اس طور پر ہے کہ یہ غلام میرے حق کے ساتھ مشغول ہے یعنی مشتری، خریدنے کی وجہ سے غلام کا مالک ہو گیا مگر اب تک اس کا شمن ادا نہیں کیا ہے پس چونکہ غلام بائع کے قبضے میں ہے اور شی ء مقبوضہ کے سلسلے میں قابض کا ہی قول معتبر ہوتا ہے اس لئے اس نے اقرار کیا ہے اسی طرح معتبر ہوگا۔ اور بائع کا اقرار اس طرح ہے کہ مشتری غلام کا تو مالک ہے مگر اس کا شمن اس کے ذمہ واجب ہے۔ مگر مشتری کا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اس سے ثمن وصول کرتا چونکہ متعذر اور ناممکن ہے اس لئے قاضی کو یہ اختیار دیا گیا کہ وہ اس غلام کو فروخت کر کے بائع کا شمن ادا کرے جیسے اگر راہن مر گیا تو مرتهن شئی مرہونہ کا زیادہ شمار ہے یعنی راہن کے مرجانے کے بعد اگر مرتہن کے لئے اپنا دین وصول کرنا متعذر ہو جائے تو شئی مرہونہ فروخت کر کے مرتبین کا دین ادا کیا جائے گا اور جیسے اگر مشتری مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے مفلس ہو کر مر گیا تو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے لیئے بیع کو فروخت کر دیا جاتا ہے اسی طرح مشتری کے ایسے طو ر نا ئب ہونے کی صورت میں کہ اس کا ٹھکانہ معلوم نہ ہو قاضی کو یہ حق دیا گیا ہے کہ وہ غلام بیچ کر بائع کا شمن ادا کرے اور اگر مشتری مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد اس طرح غائب ہوا کہ اس کا پتہ معلوم نہیں ہے تو بائع کے گواہ قبول نہیں ہوں گے اور اس کے دعوئی پر غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ بائع کا حق غلام کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں رہا بلکہ مشتری کے ذمے میں دین ہو گیا ہے پس بائع کی طرف سے مشتری پر ہینہ دین ثابت کرنے کے لئے ہوگا ۔ حالانکہ مشتری غائب ہے اور
He said: Whoever buys a slave and then disappears, while the slave is in the seller's hand, and the seller produces evidence that he sold it to him — if his disappearance is known, it is not sold for the seller's debt because the seller can reach his right without selling, and in selling there is nullification of the buyer's right. If it is not known where he is, the slave is sold and the price is paid, because the buyer's ownership is established by the seller's admission, so it appears in the way he admitted it: encumbered with his right. And when recovery from the buyer is impossible, the judge sells it for that purpose, like when a mortgagor dies, or when a buyer dies insolvent and the sold item has not been taken delivery. Unlike after delivery, because his right is no longer attached to it. Then if something remains, it is kept for the buyer because it is the substitute for his right; if there is a deficiency, he is pursued for it.
Translation: If a person buys a slave and disappears, and the slave is in the seller's hand, and the seller produces evidence that he sold this slave, then if the buyer's disappearance is known, this slave will not be sold for the seller's debt, because the seller can reach his right without selling, and selling would nullify the buyer's right. If it is not known where the buyer is, then the slave is sold and the seller's price is paid, because the buyer's ownership is established by the seller's admission, so it appears in the way he admitted: encumbered with his right. And when recovery from the buyer becomes impossible, the judge sells the slave for his right, like when a mortgagor dies, or when the buyer dies insolvent before taking delivery. Unlike after delivery, because the seller's right is no longer attached to it. Then if something remains, it is kept for the buyer, because it is the substitute for his right; if it falls short, he is pursued.
Explanation: The case is that a man bought a slave, then the buyer disappeared before taking delivery and has not paid the price. In such a case, if the seller requests the judge to sell the slave and pay his due, the judge will not entertain the claim. But if the seller produces evidence that he sold the slave to that missing person, and he neither paid the price nor took delivery, then there are two scenarios: the buyer's disappearance is either known or unknown. If the buyer's whereabouts are known, the slave will not be sold, because the seller can recover his right without selling by going to the buyer, and selling would nullify the buyer's right. If the buyer's whereabouts are unknown, then the slave is sold and the seller's price is paid, because by the seller's admission the buyer became owner, and that admission is taken at face value: the slave is encumbered with the seller's right. Since recovery from the buyer is impossible, the judge is authorized to sell the slave, as in the case of a mortgagor who dies, or if the buyer dies insolvent before taking delivery. After delivery, however, the seller's right is no longer attached to the slave, so the judge would not sell it; the seller would have to prove his debt against the buyer in absentia.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof from the matn (Qadi al-Quduri) and its explanation. It addresses the situation where a buyer purchases a slave but disappears before taking possession and before paying the price. The seller wants the slave sold to recover his due. The ruling distinguishes two cases: (1) If the buyer's whereabouts are known, the slave is not sold because the seller can pursue the buyer directly; selling would harm the buyer's right. (2) If the buyer's whereabouts are unknown, the judge may sell the slave because the buyer's ownership is established by the seller's admission, and the seller's right is attached to the slave. The reasoning compares this to the cases of a deceased mortgagor or a buyer who dies insolvent before delivery. The proof includes analogy (qiyas) and a rationale based on necessity (ta'adhdhur). The commentary follows the order: first the matn, then a literal translation, then a detailed explanation (tashrih).

Key Terms
غیبت معروفہ (ghaybat ma'rufah): A disappearance where the buyer's whereabouts are known and accessible.
مبیع (mabi'): The sold item (here, the slave) that is still in the seller's possession and has not been delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under what condition may a judge sell a slave purchased by a missing buyer?
A) When the buyer has not paid the price.
B) When the buyer's whereabouts are unknown.
C) When the seller produces witnesses.
D) When the slave is still in the seller's possession.

Key Takeaway
A judge may sell a sold slave before delivery to recover the price only if the buyer's whereabouts are unknown; otherwise, the seller must pursue the buyer directly.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Judge's Sale of Sold Item When Buyer Disappears Before Possession
Expands Hidayah ruling on seller's recourse when buyer absconds before payment and possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the sold item before possession when the buyer disappears and apply the qiyas with rahn and bankruptcy to similar cases.

Core Idea
When the buyer disappears before payment and possession, the judge may sell the item based on necessity and qiyas.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah expands on a Hidayah ruling concerning a seller who has sold a slave but the buyer vanishes before paying the price and taking possession. The text explains when the judge may sell the slave to recover the price, drawing analogies to a pledgee selling collateral after the pledgor's death and to bankruptcy before possession. It also addresses three objections raised by commentators and provides Hanafi responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Find the specific act being ruled upon: selling the slave by the judge before the buyer has taken possession. Determine the hukm level under normal circumstances and the exceptional permission here.
Normally, selling before possession is invalid. Here it becomes permissible due to necessity.
2
Locate the dalil and qiyas
The passage uses qiyas: analogy with the case of a deceased pledgor (rahn) and a bankrupt buyer before possession. Identify the illa: inability to collect the debt directly.
Qiyas jaliy: asl case of rahn, far' case of disappearing buyer, illa = ta'addur al-istifa'.
3
Trace the distinction based on possession
Note how the ruling changes after the buyer takes possession: the seller's right becomes a mere debt, and evidence against an absent person is not accepted in Hanafi law.
After qabd, the item is no longer tied to the debt; the seller must prove the debt in court.
4
Analyze the objections and Hanafi responses
Three objections are presented: (1) evidence against absent invalid, (2) selling before possession void, (3) judgment against absent impermissible. Understand each Hanafi rebuttal.
The first answer clarifies evidence is only to remove suspicion, not for verdict. The second offers two solutions: appointing a representative or incidental sale. The third distinguishes based on the possessor's acknowledgment.
5
Apply the ruling to surplus/deficit scenarios
If the sale yields more than the debt, the surplus is held for the buyer. If less, the seller can pursue the buyer for the remainder.
This shows the judge's sale is a debt recovery mechanism, not a profit-making transaction.

طرح معتبر ہوگا۔ اور بائع کا اقرار اس طرح ہے کہ مشتری غلام کا تو مالک ہے مگر اس کا ثمن اس کے ذمہ واجب ہے۔ مگر مشتری کا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اس سے ثمن وصول کرنا متعذر اور ناممکن ہے اس لئے قاضی کو یہ اختیار دیا گیا کہ وہ اس غلام کو فروخت کر کے بائع کا ثمن ادا کرے جیسے اگر راہن مر گیا تو مرتهن شئی مرہونہ کو فروخت کرتا ہے یعنی راہن کے مرجانے کے بعد اگر مرتہن کیلئے اپنا دین وصول کرنا متعذر ہو جائے تو شئی مرہونہ فروخت کر کے مرتهن کا دین ادا کیا جائے گا اور جیسے اگر مشتری مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے مفلس ہو کر مر گیا تو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے لئے مبیع کو فروخت کر دیا جاتا ہے اسی طرح مشتری کے ایسے طور پر غائب ہونے کی صورت میں کہ اس کا ٹھکانہ معلوم نہ ہو قاضی کو یہ حق دیا گیا ہے کہ وہ غلام بیچ کر بائع کا ثمن ادا کرے اور اگر مشتری مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد اس طرح غائب ہوا کہ اس کا پتہ معلوم نہیں ہے تو بائع کے گواہ قبول نہیں ہوں گے اور اس کے دعوی پر غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ بائع کا حق غلام کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں رہا بلکہ مشتری کے ذمے میں دین ہو گیا ہے پس بائع کی طرف سے مشتری پر دین ثابت کرنے کے لئے بینہ پیش کرنا ہوگا۔ حالانکہ مشتری غائب ہے اور
ہمارے نزدیک غائب پر دین ثابت کرنے کے لئے بینہ قبول نہیں کیا جاتا۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں بائع کا بینہ قبول نہ ہوگا۔ اور اس کا دین ادا کرنے کے لئے غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا۔

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس صورت میں قاضی کو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے لئے غلام فروخت کرنے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہے اس صورت میں اگر قاضی نے غلام فروخت کر کے بائع کا ثمن ادا کر دیا اور ثمن ادا کرنے کے بعد کچھ بچ گیا مثلاً: بائع کا ثمن ایک ہزار روپیہ تھا اور قاضی نے غلام بارہ سو روپیہ میں فروخت کیا تو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے بعد دوسو روپیہ باقی رہے تو یہ دوسو روپیہ مشتری کے لئے محفوظ کر لئے جائیں گے اور واپس آنے پر اس کو دیدیئے جائیں گے، کیونکہ یہ فاضل رقم مشتری ہی کے حق یعنی غلام کا بدل ہے اور اگر غلام کا ثمن بائع کے حق سے کم پڑ گیا مثلاً بائع کا حق ایک ہزار روپیہ تھا اور قاضی نے غلام فروخت کیا آٹھ سو روپیہ میں تو یہ دو سو روپیہ بائع مشتری سے اس وقت وصول کرے گا جب بائع مشتری کو پانے میں کامیاب ہو جائے گا۔

فوائد مذکورہ مسئلے میں چند اعتراضات اور ان کے جوابات عنایہ کے حوالے سے پیش خدمت ہیں: پہلا اعتراض تو یہ ہے کہ بائع نے مشتری پر جو بینہ قائم کیا ہے مشتری کے غائب ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ اقامت بینہ علی الغائب ہے اور اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نا جائز ہے کیونکہ بینہ مدعا علیہ کے انکار کی صورت میں قائم کیا جاتا ہے اور یہاں مدعی علیہ یعنی مشتری غائب ہے اور غائب کی طرف سے انکار مجہول ہے، یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ وہ انکار کر دے اور یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ وہ انکار نہ کرے۔ دوسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی کے لئے قبضے سے پہلے مبیع یعنی غلام بیچنا جائز ہے تو گویا قبضہ سے پہلے مبیع میں تصرف کرنا جائز ہوا حالانکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ قبضہ سے پہلے مبیع میں تصرف کرنا باطل ہے۔ تیسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ قاضی کا غلام بیچنے کا فیصلہ کرنا غائب یعنی مشتری کے خلاف اس کی ملک کے زائل ہونے کا فیصلہ کرنا ہے یعنی فیصلہ کرنا کہ غلام سے مشتری کی ملک زائل ہوگئی ہے حالانکہ غائب کے خلاف اس کی ملک زائل ہونے کا فیصلہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اس قضاء علی الغائب کے ناجائز ہونے میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے، خود مشتری نے مبیع یعنی غلام پر قبضہ کیا ہو یا قبضہ نہ کیا ہو حالانکہ آپ نے فرق کیا ہے پس ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق کرنا تحکم یعنی دعوی بلا دلیل ہے۔

پہلے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے بائع کا بینہ پیش کرنا اپنے اوپر سے تہمت دور کرنے کے لئے ہے نہ کہ قاضی کے فیصلہ دینے کے لئے، قاضی تو بائع یعنی قابض کے اقرار کرنے پر فیصلہ کرتا ہے اور اس میں مدعا علیہ کے انکار کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ پس یہ اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نہ کہلائے گی۔ اور جب اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نہیں ہوا تو نا جائز بھی نہ ہوگا بلکہ جائز ہوگا۔ دوسرے اعتراض کا ایک جواب تو یہ ہے کہ بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ قاضی ایک آدمی کو مقرر کر دے گا تا کہ وہ مشتری کے لئے اس غلام پر قبضہ کر کے پھر اس کو فروخت کر دے کیونکہ قاضی کی بیع مشتری کی بیع کے مانند ہے۔ پس جس طرح مشتری، قبضہ کر کے اس کو بیچنے کا مجاز تھا اسی طرح قاضی بھی مجاز ہوگا۔ اور جب مذکورہ غلام قبضہ کے بعد بیچا گیا تو قبل القبض بیچنا اور تصرف کرنا لازم نہ آیا۔ دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں قاضی کے غلام کو فروخت کرنے سے بیع مقصود نہیں ہے بلکہ بائع کے حق کو ادا کرنے کیلئے بائع پر نظر شفقت کرنا ہے، اور یہ بات دوسری ہے کہ بیع ضمناً حاصل ہو جاتی ہے اور بار ہا ایسا ہوتا ہے کہ ایک چیز تو ضمناً ثابت ہو جاتی ہے مگر قصدا نہیں۔
Chapter of Salam. It is possible and in selling there is nullification of the buyer's right. And if it is not known where the buyer is, then the slave is sold and the seller's price is paid, because the buyer's ownership is manifested by the seller's acknowledgment. So as he acknowledged, i.e., encumbered with his right, so it will be manifested. And when receiving the seller's right from the buyer becomes difficult, the judge will sell the slave for that right, like when a pledgor dies, and when the buyer dies bankrupt while the sold item has not been taken possession of. Contrary to after possession, because the seller's right is no longer attached to the sold item. Then if something remains, it will be kept for the buyer, because it is the substitute of the buyer's right. And if it falls short, the buyer will be pursued. Explanation of the case: A man bought a slave, then the buyer disappeared before taking possession of the slave, and the buyer has not yet paid the price. In such a case, if the seller requests the judge to sell the mentioned slave and pay his price, the judge will not pay attention to the seller's claim. Yes, if the seller produces witnesses that he sold his slave to a certain person who is absent, but he neither paid the price nor took possession, then there are two situations: the buyer's whereabouts are known or unknown. If the buyer's location is known, then the slave will not be sold to pay the seller's debt, because in that case the seller can collect his right without selling the slave by going to the buyer and collecting the price, and selling the slave would nullify the buyer's right, and nullifying someone's right is not correct. So when selling the slave necessitates nullifying the buyer's right, and the seller can collect his right without selling, then the slave will not be sold to pay the seller's debt. And if the buyer's location is unknown, then in that case the mentioned slave is sold and the seller's price is paid, because it became manifest from the seller's acknowledgment that the buyer is the owner of this slave. So as he acknowledged, the buyer's ownership will be manifested accordingly. And the seller's acknowledgment is that this slave is encumbered with my right, i.e., the buyer became owner of the slave by purchasing but has not yet paid its price. Since the slave is in the seller's possession, and in matters of a possessed thing, the possessor's statement is authoritative, so his acknowledgment is authoritative accordingly. And the seller's acknowledgment is that the buyer is the owner of the slave but its price is obligatory upon him. But because collecting the price from him is difficult and impossible due to unknown location, the judge is given authority to sell this slave and pay the seller's price, just as if a pledgor dies, the pledgee sells the mortgaged property, i.e., after the pledgor's death, if it becomes difficult for the pledgee to collect his debt, the mortgaged property is sold and the pledgee's debt is paid. And just as if the buyer dies bankrupt before taking possession of the sold item, the sold item is sold to pay the seller's price, similarly in the case of the buyer's disappearance such that his location is unknown, the judge is given this right to sell the slave and pay the seller's price. And if the buyer disappears after taking possession of the sold item such that his trace is unknown, then the seller's witnesses will not be accepted and the slave will not be sold on his claim, because the seller's right is no longer attached to the slave but became a debt in the buyer's liability. So the seller must produce evidence to prove the debt against the buyer. And in our view, evidence to prove a debt against an absent person is not accepted. Therefore, in this case, the seller's evidence will not be accepted, and the slave will not be sold to pay his debt. The author of Hidayah says: In the case where the judge is given authority to sell the slave to pay the seller's price, if the judge sells the slave and pays the seller's price, and after paying the price something remains, e.g., the seller's price was one thousand rupees and the judge sold the slave for twelve hundred rupees, then after paying the seller's price, two hundred rupees remain. These two hundred rupees will be preserved for the buyer and given to him when he returns, because this surplus is the substitute of the buyer's right, i.e., the value of the slave. And if the price of the slave falls short of the seller's right, e.g., the seller's right was one thousand rupees and the judge sold the slave for eight hundred rupees, then the seller will collect these two hundred rupees from the buyer when he is able to find him. Benefits: In the mentioned issue, there are several objections and their answers presented with reference to 'Inayah. The first objection is that the seller established evidence against the buyer, and due to the buyer's absence, this is establishing evidence against an absent person, and establishing evidence against an absent person is impermissible, because evidence is established in the case of denial by the defendant, and here the defendant, the buyer, is absent, and denial from an absent person is unknown; it is possible he may deny and possible he may not deny. The second objection is that when it is permissible for the judge to sell the sold item before possession, it means it is permissible to dispose of the sold item before possession, whereas it has already been stated that disposing of the sold item before possession is void. The third objection is that the judge's decision to sell the slave is a decision against the absent buyer regarding the removal of his ownership, i.e., deciding that the buyer's ownership of the slave is removed, whereas it is not permissible to decide removal of ownership against an absent person, and there is no difference in the impermissibility of this judgment against the absent person whether the buyer took possession of the sold item or not, whereas you made a distinction, so making a distinction between them is arbitrary, i.e., a claim without proof. Answer to the first objection: The seller's presenting evidence is to remove suspicion from himself, not for the judge to give a verdict. The judge decides based on the seller's (the possessor's) acknowledgment, and in that there is no need for the defendant's denial. So this will not be called establishing evidence against an absent person. And when it is not establishing evidence against an absent person, it will not be impermissible but rather permissible. One answer to the second objection is that some scholars said that the judge will appoint a person to take possession of the slave on behalf of the buyer and then sell it, because the judge's sale is like the buyer's sale. So just as the buyer was authorized to take possession and sell it, similarly the judge will be authorized. And when the mentioned slave is sold after possession, then selling and disposing before possession does not occur. Another answer is that here the purpose of the judge selling the slave is not the sale itself, but rather to show kindness to the seller by recovering his right, and it is another matter that the sale occurs incidentally, and many times a thing is proven incidentally but not intentionally.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from 'Ashraf al-Hidayah' on a specific ruling from the Hidayah regarding the sale of a slave when the buyer disappears before taking possession and before paying the price. The commentator first states the general ruling: if the buyer's whereabouts are unknown, the slave may be sold by the judge to pay the seller; if known, it should not be sold because the seller can directly claim the price. The core reasoning is that the seller's acknowledgment of the buyer's ownership, combined with the buyer's inability to pay due to absence, makes it permissible to sell the slave analogically (qiyas) to two cases: (1) a pledgor's death where the pledgee sells the collateral, and (2) a buyer's bankruptcy before possession where the seller can repossess the goods. The passage then distinguishes between disappearance before and after possession: after possession, the seller's right is no longer attached to the item but becomes a debt, and since Hanafi law does not accept evidence against an absent person to prove a debt, the slave cannot be sold. The text also addresses the fate of surplus or deficit in the sale proceeds. Finally, three objections are presented and answered. The first objection concerns the validity of evidence against an absent person; the answer clarifies that the evidence is only to remove suspicion from the seller, not to prove the case to the judge—the judge relies on the seller's acknowledgment as possessor. The second objection about selling before possession is answered by noting that the judge can appoint someone to take possession first, or that the sale is incidental to the primary aim of recovering the seller's right. The third objection about judging against an absent person is refuted by distinguishing that here the judge is not making a decision against the absent buyer but rather acting on the possessor's acknowledgment. The commentary follows the text's order: ruling, explanation of the case, application of qiyas, distinction with post-possession, handling of surplus/deficit, and then the objections and answers. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or legal possession of the sold item.
اقامت بینہ علی الغائب (iqamat bayyinah 'ala al-gha'ib): Producing evidence against an absent person in court.
تصرف قبل القبض (tasarruf qabl al-qabd): Disposal of the sold item by the buyer before taking possession.
ثمن (thaman): Price or payment in a sale contract.
قضاء علی الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment against an absent person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the critical factor that determines whether the judge may sell the slave before possession?
A) Whether the seller has witnesses.
B) Whether the buyer's location is known or unknown.
C) Whether the slave is of high value.
D) Whether the seller is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
When the buyer disappears before payment and possession and his location is unknown, the Hanafi judge may sell the sold item to recover the price, by analogy with rahn and bankruptcy.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Qadi Selling Absent Buyer's Slave: Ruling and Refutation of Objections
Ruling on qadi's sale of absent buyer's slave with objections and answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a qadi selling an absent buyer's slave to satisfy the seller and refute three common objections using the arguments from al-Hidayah and al-Inayah.

Core Idea
The qadi may sell the slave of an absent buyer to satisfy the seller, based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling where a qadi is permitted to sell a slave in the possession of the seller to recover a debt owed by an absent buyer. The passage from al-Hidayah presents the ruling, then addresses three objections raised against it, providing detailed answers from al-Inayah. Understanding these objections and their refutations is key to appreciating how Hanafi jurists apply istihsan to resolve practical conflicts between general principles and specific needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its context
Begin by identifying the specific situation: a seller has a debt claim against an absent buyer, and the purchased slave remains in the seller's possession (the buyer has not taken delivery). The qadi is given authority to sell that slave to pay the seller. Note that this is an exception to the general rule that testimony is not accepted against an absent person.
2
Understand the treatment of surplus or deficit after sale
The passage explains two scenarios: if the sale price exceeds the debt, the surplus is preserved for the buyer; if it falls short, the seller can recover the shortfall later when he catches the buyer. This demonstrates the qadi's role as a trustee.
3
Analyze each objection and its Hanafi answer
Three objections are presented: (1) establishing evidence against an absent person is impermissible, (2) selling before possession is prohibited, (3) the qadi's sale amounts to judgment against an absent person, which is not allowed. For each, identify the Hanafi response and the principle behind it.
The answers rely on: (1) the qadi acts on confession, not evidence; (2) the sale is incidental, or a proxy can take possession first; (3) the judgment is actually against the present seller, not the absent buyer.
4
Recognize the role of istihsan
This ruling is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding the general rule. The general rule forbids judgment against an absent person and sale before possession, but the need to protect the seller's right justifies the exception.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle used to achieve justice in cases where strict analogy (qiyas) would lead to hardship.

ہمارے نزدیک غائب پر دین ثابت کرنے کے لئے بینہ قبول نہیں کیا جاتا۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں بائع کا بینہ قبول نہ ہوگا۔ اور اس کا دین ادا کرنے کے لئے غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا۔

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس صورت میں قاضی کو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے لئے غلام فروخت کرنے کا اختیار دیا گیا ہے اس صورت میں اگر قاضی نے غلام فروخت کر کے بائع کا ثمن ادا کر دیا اور ثمن ادا کرنے کے بعد کچھ بچ گیا مثلا : بائع کا ثمن ایک ہزار روپیہ تھا اور قاضی نے غلام بارہ سو روپیہ میں فروخت کیا تو بائع کا ثمن ادا کرنے کے بعد دوسو روپیہ باقی رہے تو یہ دوسور و پیہ مشتری کے لئے محفوظ کر لئے جائیں گے اور واپس آنے پر اس کو دیدیئے جائیں گے، کیونکہ یہ فاضل رقم مشتری ہی کے حق یعنی غلام کا بدل ہے اور اگر غلام کا ثمن بائع کے حق سے کم پڑ گیا مثلا با ئع کا حق ایک ہزار روپیہ تھا اور قاضی نے غلام فروخت کیا آٹھ سورو پیہ میں تو یہ دوسور و پیہ بائع مشتری سے اس وقت وصول کرے گا جب بائع مشتری کو پانے میں کامیاب ہو جائے گا

فوائد مذکورہ مسئلے میں چند اعتراضات اور ان کے جوابات عنایہ کے حوالے سے پیش خدمت ہیں: پہلا اعتراض تو یہ ہے کہ بائع نے مشتری پر جو بینہ قائم کیا ہے مشتری کے غائب ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ اقامت بینہ علی الغائب ہے اور اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نا جائز ہے کیونکہ بینہ مدعلی علیہ کے انکار کی صورت میں قائم کیا جاتا ہے اور یہاں مدعی علیہ یعنی مشتری غائب ہے اور غائب کی طرف سے انکار مجہول ہے، یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ وہ انکار کر دے اور یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ وہ انکار نہ کرے۔ دوسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی کے لئے قبضے سے پہلے مبیع یعنی غلام بیچنا جائز ہے تو گویا قبضہ سے پہلے مبیع میں تصرف کرنا جائز ہوا حالانکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ قبضہ سے پہلے مبیع میں تصرف کرنا باطل ہے۔ تیسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ قاضی کا غلام بیچنے کا فیصلہ کرنا غائب یعنی مشتری کے خلاف اس کی ملک کے زائل ہونے کا فیصلہ کرنا ہے یعنی فیصلہ کرنا کہ غلام سے مشتری کی ملک زائل ہوگئی ہے حالانکہ غائب کے خلاف اس کی ملک زائل ہونے کا فیصلہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور اس قضاء علی الغائب کے ناجائز ہونے میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے، خود شتری نے مبیع یعنی غلام پر قبضہ کیا ہو یا قبضہ نہ کیا ہو حالانکہ آپ نے فرق کیا ہے پس ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق کرنا تحکم یعنی دعوی بلا دلیل ہے۔

پہلے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے بائع کا بینہ پیش کرنا اپنے اوپر سے تہمت دور کرنے کے لئے ہے نہ کہ قاضی کے فیصلہ دینے کے لئے، قاضی تو بائع یعنی قابض کے اقرار کرنے پر فیصلہ کرتا ہے اور اس میں مدعلی علیہ کے انکار کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ پس یہ اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نہ کہلائے گی ۔ اور جب اقامت بینہ علی الغائب نہیں ہوا تو نا جائز بھی نہ ہوگا بلکہ جائز ہوگا۔ دوسرے اعتراض کا ایک جواب تو یہ ہے کہ بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ قاضی ایک آدمی کو مقرر کر دے گا تا کہ وہ مشتری کے لئے اُس غلام پر قبضہ کر کے پھر اس کو فروخت کر دے کیونکہ قاضی کی بیع مشتری کی بیع کے مانند ہے۔ پس جس طرح مشتری، قبضہ کر کے اس کو بیچنے کا مجاز تھا اسی طرح قاضی بھی مجاز ہوگا۔ اور جب مذکورہ غلام قبضہ کے بعد بیچا گیا تو قبل القبض بیچنا اور تصرف کرنا لازم نہ آیا۔ دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں قاضی کے غلام کو فروخت کرنے سے بیع مقصود نہیں ہے بلکہ بائع کے حق کو نفادہ کرنے کیلئے بائع پر نظر شفقت کرنا ہے، اور یہ بات دوسری ہے کہ بیع ضمنا حاصل ہو جاتی ہے اور بار ہا ایسا ہوتا ہے کہ ایک چیز تو ضمنا ثابت ہو جاتی ہے مگر قصدا ثابت نہیں ہوتی ۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی بیع قبل القبض اگر چہ ضمنا ثابت ہو جاتی ہے مگر قصد اثابت نہیں ہوتی۔ تیسرے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کا غلام کو فروخت کرنے کا فیصلہ کرنا قضاء علی الغائب نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ قضاء علی الحاضر ہے، یعنی قاضی نے بائع (حاضر) کے اُس چیز کے اقرار کے بارے میں فیصلہ دیا ہے جو اس کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اور یہ اقرار کی وجہ سے قضاء علی الحاضر اسی وقت ہوگا جب کہ مشتری نے مبیع یعنی غلام پر قبضہ نہ کیا ہو۔ مگر جب مشتری نے قبضہ کر لیا تو یہ بائع کے اقرار کی وجہ سے مقبوضہ چیز کے بارے میں قضاء علی الحاضر نہ ہو گا۔ پس اس تقریر سے واضح ہو گیا کہ مشتری کے مبیع پر قبضہ کرنے اور قبضہ نہ کرنے کی صورتوں میں بہت بڑا فرق ہے اور جب فرق ہے تو فرق کا دعوی کرنا تحکم یعنی دعوئی بلا دلیل بھی نہ ہوگا۔ (احقر الناس تجمیل احمد عفی عنہ)
According to us, testimony is not accepted to establish a debt against an absent person. Therefore, in this case, the seller's testimony will not be accepted, and his slave will not be sold to pay his debt. The author of al-Hidayah says: In the situation where the qadi has been given authority to sell the slave to pay the seller's price, if the qadi sells the slave and pays the seller's price, and after paying the price some excess remains—for example, the seller's price was one thousand rupees and the qadi sold the slave for twelve hundred rupees—then after paying the seller's price, two hundred rupees remain; these two hundred rupees will be preserved for the buyer and given to him when he returns, because this surplus is the buyer's right, i.e., the price of the slave. And if the slave's price falls short of the seller's right—for example, the seller's right was one thousand rupees and the qadi sold the slave for eight hundred rupees—then the seller will recover these two hundred rupees from the buyer when the seller succeeds in capturing the buyer.

Benefits: In this mentioned issue, there are several objections and their answers presented from Inayah: The first objection is that the seller's establishment of testimony against the buyer is, due to the buyer's absence, an establishment of testimony against the absent, and establishing testimony against the absent is impermissible because testimony is established in the case of the defendant's denial, and here the defendant (the buyer) is absent, and the denial from the absent is unknown—it is possible that he may deny or may not deny. The second objection: When it is permissible for the qadi to sell the commodity (the slave) before possession, then it is as if acting in the commodity before possession is permissible, although it has already passed that acting in the commodity before possession is invalid. The third objection: The qadi's judgment to sell the slave is a judgment against the absent (the buyer) that his ownership has been removed, i.e., a judgment that the buyer's ownership has been removed from the slave, whereas a judgment against the absent that his ownership has been removed is not permissible, and there is no difference in the impermissibility of this judgment against the absent, whether the buyer himself has taken possession of the commodity (the slave) or not, although you have made a distinction. Therefore, making a distinction between the two is arbitrary (a claim without proof).

Answer to the first objection: The seller's presentation of testimony is to remove suspicion from himself, not for the qadi to give a verdict. The qadi gives a verdict based on the seller's (the possessor's) confession, and there is no need for the defendant's denial. Thus, this will not be called establishment of testimony against the absent. And when it is not establishment of testimony against the absent, it will not be impermissible, but rather permissible. Answer to the second objection: One answer is that some scholars said: The qadi will appoint a person to take possession of that slave for the buyer and then sell it, because the qadi's sale is like the buyer's sale. Just as the buyer was authorized to take possession and then sell it, similarly the qadi will be authorized. And when the said slave is sold after possession, selling and acting before possession does not occur. Another answer: Here selling the slave by the qadi is not intended as a sale; rather, it is to fulfill the seller's right out of consideration for the seller. And this is another matter: the sale occurs incidentally. Many times it happens that a thing is proven incidentally but not intentionally. So similarly here, sale before possession, though proven incidentally, is not intended primarily. Answer to the third objection: The qadi's judgment to sell the slave is not a judgment against the absent; rather, it is a judgment against the present. That is, the qadi gave a verdict about the confession of the seller (who is present) concerning the thing that is in his possession. And this judgment against the present due to confession will occur only when the buyer has not taken possession of the commodity (the slave). But when the buyer has taken possession, this will not be a judgment against the present concerning the possessed thing due to the seller's confession. Thus, from this exposition, it becomes clear that there is a great difference between the cases where the buyer has taken possession of the commodity and where he has not. And when there is a difference, claiming a distinction is not arbitrary (a claim without proof). (The humblest of people, Tajmeel Ahmad, may he be pardoned)
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the authority of a qadi to sell a slave that is in the possession of the seller (as a pledge or retained item) to satisfy the seller's debt from an absent buyer. The base ruling states that testimony is not accepted against an absent person, so normally the seller cannot prove his right and the slave should not be sold. However, the author of al-Hidayah introduces a special scenario where the qadi is given authority to sell. The passage then addresses the disposition of surplus or deficit after sale. The commentator then presents three objections that might arise against this ruling, likely from opponents (possibly from other schools or hypothetical critics), and refutes them. The first objection concerns establishing testimony against an absent person; the answer clarifies that the qadi acts on the seller's confession, not on testimony against the absent. The second objection is about selling before possession; two answers are given: one that the qadi can have a proxy take possession first, another that the sale is incidental, not primary. The third objection is about judgment against an absent person removing his property; the answer distinguishes between the case where the buyer has taken possession (then it would be against the absent) and where he hasn't (then it is against the present based on the seller's confession). The passage concludes by affirming the distinction made by the Hanafi school. The overall ruling is a concession (istihsan) for the sake of the seller's need, allowing the qadi to facilitate payment.

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence/testimony presented in court to prove a claim
قضاء علی الغائب (qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib): Judgment against an absent person without their presence or defense
قبض (qabḍ): Physical possession or taking delivery of the purchased item
اقرار (iqrār): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or obligation
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference overriding strict analogy (qiyas) to achieve equity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the purpose of the seller presenting evidence (bayyinah) in the qadi's court?
A) To prove the absent buyer's liability
B) To remove suspicion from himself regarding his claim
C) To force the qadi to issue a judgment against the absent
D) To establish the amount of the debt exactly

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a qadi to sell an absent buyer's property in the seller's possession to satisfy the seller's debt, using istihsan to override strict rules against judgment absent and sale before possession.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Two Buyers: One Absent — Can the Present Buyer Take Full Possession?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on present buyer's right after paying full price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa & Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf on this issue, apply the concept of mudtarr (compulsion) to justify recovery, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a joint sale is a single contract, necessity allows the present buyer to pay all and recover later.

Imagine two people jointly purchase a slave. One disappears before paying his share. The seller refuses to hand over the slave until full payment. The present buyer wishes to pay the entire price, take the slave, and later recover from his absent partner. Is this permissible? The Hanafi masters disagree: Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say yes, he is compelled; Abu Yusuf says no, he is a volunteer. This lesson dissects the arguments and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Legal Question
Two buyers jointly purchase a slave in a single transaction (safqah wahidah). One buyer disappears before paying his share. The seller retains the slave due to unpaid price. Can the present buyer pay the full price, take the entire slave, and later recover from the absent co-buyer?
2
State the Position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They allow the present buyer to pay the full price, take possession of the entire slave, and recover his partner's share later. The present buyer is mudtarr (compelled) because he cannot benefit from his own share without paying the full price.
3
State the Position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the present buyer can only take his own share. The amount paid for the absent partner is a voluntary charity (mutatawwi') because he paid another's debt without permission. Therefore, he cannot recover that amount nor take the absent's share.
4
Examine the Proofs and Analogies
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue the sale is one unit (safqah wahidah), giving the seller the right of retention until full payment. The present buyer is compelled and can recover, analogous to the lender of a pawn (mu'ir al-rahn) and an agent who pays from his own money (wakil bil-shira').
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Thamarah al-Khilaf
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Practical consequence: under this view, the present buyer can secure his right and recover; under Abu Yusuf, he loses the extra payment and only gets his share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری دو آدمی ہوں ان میں سے ایک غائب ہو گیا تو حاضر کل ثمن ادا کرے اور اس غلام پر قبضہ کرلے، دوسرا آجائے تو اس کا حصہ ثمن لیکر لوٹا دے

فان كان المشترى اثنين فغاب احدهما فللحاضر ان يدفع الثمن كله ويقبضه واذا حضر الآخر يأخذ نصيبه حتى ينقد شريكه الثمن وهو قول ابى حنيفة ومحمد وقال ابويوسف اذا دفع الحاضر الثمن كله لم يقبض الا نصيبه وكان متطوعا بما ادى عن صاحبه لانه قضی دین غیره بغیر امره فلا يرجع عليه وهو اجنبى عن نصيب صاحبه فلا يقبضه ولهما انه مضطر فيه لانه لا يمكنه الانتفاع بنصيبه الا باداء جميع الثمن لان البيع صفقة واحدة وله حق الحبس ما بقى شيء منه والمضطر يرجع كمعير الرهن واذا كان له ان يرجع عليه كان له الخبس عنه الى ان يستوفي حقه كالوكيل بالشراء اذا قضى الثمن من مال نفسه

ترجمہ . پس اگر مشتری دو آدمی ہوں پھر ان دونوں میں سے ایک غائب ہو گیا تو حاضر کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ پورا ثمن ادا کرے اور غلام پر قبضہ کر لے اور جب دوسرا حاضر ہوا تو وہ اپنا حصہ نہیں لے گا، یہاں تک کہ اپنے شریک کو اپنا حصہ ثمن ادا کرے۔ اور یہ ابوحنیفہ اور امام محمد کا قول ہے۔ اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا کہ جب حاضر نے پورا ثمن ادا کر دیا تو وہ فقط اپنے حصہ پر قبضہ کر سکتا ہے۔ اور جو کچھ اپنے ساتھی کی طرف سے ادا کیا ہے اس میں احسان کرنے والا ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے دوسرے کا قرضہ بغیر اس کے حکم کے ادا کیا ہے اس لئے وہ اس سے واپس نہیں لے سکتا۔ اور چونکہ وہ اپنے ساتھی کے حصہ سے اجنبی ہے اس لئے اس کے حصہ پر قبضہ بھی نہیں کر سکتا ہے اور طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے که شریک حاضر پورا ثمن دینے میں مجبور اور لاچار ہے کیونکہ اس کے لئے اپنے حصے سے نفع اٹھانا ممکن نہیں ہے۔ مگر پورا ثمن ادا کر کے اس لئے کہ بیع صفقہ واحدہ ہے اور جب تک ثمن میں سے کچھ باقی ہو بائع کو مبیع روکنے کا حق حاصل ہے اور مضطر اور مجبور واپس لے سکتا ہے جیسے رہن کو عاریہ دینے والا اور جب شریک حاضر غائب سے اس کا حصہ واپس لے سکتا ہے تو اس کو غائب سے مبیع روکنے کا بھی حق ہے یہاں تک کہ شریک حاضر اپنا پورا حق وصول کرلے جیسے: وکیل بالشراء جب اپنے مال سے ثمن ادا کر دے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر دو آدمیوں نے مل کر ایک غلام خریدا اور پھر ان دونوں میں سے ایک مشتری ثمن ادا کرنے سے پہلے غائب ہو گیا تو شریک حاضر اپنے حصہ پر قبضہ کا مالک اسی وقت ہو گا جب پورا ثمن ادا کر دے۔ پس اگر شریک حاضر نے پورا ثمن ادا کر دیا تو طرفین اور ابو یوسف کے درمیان چند باتوں میں اختلاف ہے:
If the buyers are two and one of them becomes absent, then the present buyer may pay the entire price and take possession of the slave, and when the other returns, he will take his share only after paying his partner’s share of the price. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: When the present buyer pays the entire price, he takes possession only of his share, and he is a volunteer (mutatawwi') in what he paid on behalf of his companion, because he paid another's debt without his permission, so he cannot recover from him; and he is a stranger to his companion's share, so he cannot take possession of it. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) proof is that he is compelled (mudtarr) in it, because he cannot benefit from his share except by paying the entire price, since the sale is one single transaction (safqah wahidah) and the seller has the right of retention (haqb al-habs) as long as anything of the price remains. And the compelled person can recover (like the lender of a pawn), and if he can recover from him, he has the right to retain the thing from him until he recovers his right, like an agent for purchase when he pays the price from his own money.

Translation: So if the buyers are two, then one of them becomes absent, the present buyer has the option to pay the entire price and take possession of the slave, and when the other appears, he will not take his share until he pays his share of the price to his partner. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. And Imam Abu Yusuf said: When the present buyer pays the entire price, he can only take possession of his share, and whatever he paid on behalf of his companion is an act of charity, because he paid another’s debt without his permission, so he cannot recover from him; and since he is a stranger to his companion's share, he cannot take possession of it. The proof of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the present partner is compelled and helpless because it is not possible for him to benefit from his share except by paying the entire price, because the sale is one single transaction, and the seller has the right to retain the commodity as long as any part of the price remains. And the compelled person can recover, like the one who gives a pawn as a loan (mu'ir al-rahn). And when the present partner can recover his share from the absent, he also has the right to retain the commodity from the absent until the present partner recovers his full right, like an agent for purchase when he pays the price from his own money. Explanation of the case: If two persons jointly buy a slave and then one of them, before paying the price, becomes absent, the present partner will be owner of possession of his share only when he pays the full price. So, if the present partner pays the full price, there is disagreement between the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf on several points:
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific legal scenario: two persons jointly purchase a slave as a single transaction. One buyer disappears before paying his share of the price. The present buyer wants to take delivery of the slave but the seller can retain it until full payment. The question is: may the present buyer pay the full price and take possession of the entire slave, and then recover the absent buyer's share from him later? The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side, and Abu Yusuf on the other. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the present buyer is compelled (mudtarr) because he cannot benefit from his own share without paying the entire price, since the sale is one single contract and the seller has the right of retention (habs) until full payment. Therefore, the present buyer can pay the whole price, take possession of the whole slave, and then claim reimbursement from the absent buyer, akin to a compelled person recovering from another. They analogize to various cases: the lender of a pawn (mu'ir al-rahn) who can recover, and an agent for purchase who pays from his own money and then recovers. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he says the present buyer can only take his own share, and the amount he paid for his partner is a voluntary charity (mutatawwi'), because he paid another's debt without permission. Thus, he cannot recover that amount, nor can he take possession of the absent partner's share. The passage ends by noting that there are several points of disagreement, but the text cuts off. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is generally the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, due to the principle of necessity and the unity of the sale contract.

Key Terms
صفقہ واحدہ (ṣafqah wāḥidah): A single transaction or contract that binds all parties together
مضطر (muḍṭarr): One who is compelled by necessity to act in a certain way
وکیل بالشراء (wakīl bil-shirā'): An agent authorized to purchase on behalf of another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The present buyer cannot recover because he paid voluntarily.
B) The present buyer can recover because he is mudtarr and the contract is unified.
C) The present buyer can only take his share and must donate the extra payment.
D) The present buyer must wait until the absent returns to pay.

Key Takeaway
In a joint purchase with a unified contract, the present buyer may pay the full price, take the entire chattel, and recover the absent co-buyer's share because he is compelled by necessity.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Present Buyer Paying Full Price When Co-Buyer Is Absent: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on present buyer's rights when co-buyer is absent in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding the present buyer's rights in a joint sale with an absent co-buyer, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequences) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Necessity (idtarar) transforms a volunteer into a claimant: the mudtar can recover and retain.

In a joint purchase where one buyer is absent, the present buyer faces a dilemma: he cannot benefit from his share unless the full price is paid, because the sale is a single transaction (safqah wahidah). This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Tarafan) and Abu Yusuf over whether the present buyer may pay the absent's share, take the entire goods, and later recover the cost. The dispute turns on whether the present buyer is a volunteer (mutabarri") or compelled (mudtar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two buyers jointly purchase a slave in a salam contract. One buyer becomes absent before paying his share. The present buyer wishes to take possession of the entire slave. Determine the core legal question: can the present buyer pay the full price, take the slave, and later recover the absent's share of the price?
The sale is a single transaction (safqah wahidah).
2
State the position of al-Tarafan (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
The present buyer may pay the full price, take possession, recover the absent's share from him, and retain the absent's share of the merchandise as security until reimbursement. Their reasoning: the present buyer is compelled (mudtar) because he cannot benefit from his share without paying the entire price; the seller has the right of retention (haqq al-habs) over the whole slave until the full price is paid.
They analogize to the lender of a pawn (mu'ir al-rahn) and the purchase agent (wakil bil-shira').
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
The present buyer only takes his own share; he is a volunteer (mutatawwi") in paying the absent's share because he paid without command. Therefore, he cannot reclaim the absent's share from him, cannot retain the absent's share, and the seller cannot be compelled to accept the absent's share or hand over the absent's portion.
Abu Yusuf argues that paying another's debt without command is a gratuitous payment with no right of recourse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Al-Tarafan respond that the present buyer is not a volunteer due to necessity. His share is tied to the whole in a single transaction; the seller's retention right forces him to pay the full price. A compelled person (mudtar) can reclaim what he paid, like the lender of a pawn who redeems his pawn and recovers from the pawner. Similarly, the purchase agent who pays from his own money can reclaim and retain.
The key distinction: necessity (idtarar) elevates the payer from volunteer to compelled claimant.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: Under Abu Yusuf, the present buyer must either wait for the absent buyer (losing benefit) or involve the seller; under al-Tarafan, he can proceed unilaterally and later recover. The mufta bihi position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is supported by stronger analogy and practical necessity.
The text states: 'وهو قول ابى حنيفة ومحمد' emphasizing this as the relied-upon view.

رکھ دیا اور قرضہ لے لیا۔ پھر مستعیر یعنی راہن مفلس ہو گیا یا غائب ہو گیا اور معیر یعنی صاحب مرہونہ کے مالک نے مرتہن کا قرضہ ادا کر کے اپنی چیز کو چھڑالیا تو معیر ، راہن سے وہ
If the buyers are two, and one of them becomes absent, then the present one may pay the entire price and take possession, and when the other comes, he takes his share only after paying his partner the price. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: When the present one pays the full price, he only takes possession of his own share, and he is a volunteer regarding what he paid on behalf of his companion, because he paid the debt of another without his command, so he cannot reclaim it from him; and he is a stranger regarding his companion's share, so he cannot take possession of it. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) argument is that he is compelled in this, because he cannot benefit from his share except by paying the entire price, since the sale is a single transaction, and the seller has the right to retain the merchandise as long as any part of the price remains; and one who is compelled can reclaim what he paid, like the lender of a pawn (when he redeems it). And if he has the right to reclaim it from him, then he has the right to retain the merchandise from him until he recovers his due, like the agent for purchase when he pays the price from his own wealth...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about a joint sale (safqah wahidah) in a salam contract. The case: two buyers jointly purchase a slave. One buyer becomes absent before paying his share. The present buyer wishes to take possession of the entire slave. The key question is whether the present buyer can pay the full price, take the slave, and later recover the absent buyer's share. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (known as al-Tarafan) hold that the present buyer is compelled to pay the full price because the sale is a single transaction (safqah wahidah) and the seller has the right of retention (haqq al-habs) until the entire price is paid. Since the present buyer cannot benefit from his share without paying the whole, he is considered compelled (mudtar) and therefore has the right to recover the absent buyer's share from him, and also to retain the absent buyer's share of the slave as security until reimbursement. Imam Abu Yusuf differs: he considers the present buyer a volunteer (mutatawwi') in paying the absent buyer's share, because he paid without being commanded. Hence, he cannot reclaim nor retain. The passage discusses three practical consequences: (1) whether the seller can be compelled to accept the absent buyer's share of the price; (2) whether the seller can be compelled to hand over the absent buyer's share of the slave; (3) whether the present buyer can recover the absent's share of the price and retain his share. Imam Abu Yusuf rejects all three; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad accept them. The passage then provides analogies: the compelled person is like the lender of a pawn (mu'ir al-rahn) who redeems the pawn and can recover; similarly, the agent for purchase (wakil bil-shira') who pays from his own money can reclaim. The relied-upon position for fatwa is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is the stronger proof based on necessity and the nature of a single transaction.

Key Terms
صفقة واحدة (safqah wahidah): A single transaction where the sale is indivisible; the entire contract is one unit.
مضطر (mudtar): One who is compelled by necessity to act; here, the present buyer is compelled to pay the full price to benefit from his own share.
متبرع (mutabarri'): A volunteer who acts without being legally obligated; Abu Yusuf considers the present buyer a volunteer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling regarding the present buyer's ability to retain the absent buyer's share of the purchased slave?
A) He cannot retain it and must hand it over immediately.
B) He can retain it until the absent buyer pays his share of the price.
C) He can only retain it if the seller agrees.
D) He must return it to the seller.

Key Takeaway
In a joint sale with an absent co-buyer, the present buyer is considered compelled (mudtar) and may pay the full price, take possession, recover the absent's share, and retain the absent's share as security until reimbursement.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Present Partner's Rights: Compelled Payer vs Volunteer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compulsion to pay absent partner's share and reclaim it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Imam Abu Yusuf on the rights of a present partner who pays the full price, and apply the principle of safqa wahida to determine when a payer is compelled (majbur) versus a volunteer (mutabarri').

Core Idea
A partner compelled to pay the absent partner's share due to a single bargain can reclaim it, unlike a volunteer.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the rights of a present partner who buys jointly with an absent partner. The focal point is whether the present partner is considered compelled (majbur) or a volunteer (mutabarri') when paying the absent partner's share. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining rights of recourse and retention. We will examine the arguments of the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) against Imam Abu Yusuf, and the analogies used.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the transaction and sub-issues
The scenario: two partners buy a slave jointly. The present partner pays the full price. Three sub-issues arise: (1) Can the seller be compelled to accept the absent partner's share? (2) Can the seller be compelled to hand over the absent partner's share to the present partner? (3) Can the present partner reclaim the half-price from the absent partner and withhold the slave?
2
2. State the position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
On all three issues, the two hold that the seller can be compelled, and the present partner has the right to reclaim and withhold.
This is the majority Hanafi view (Zahir al-Riwayah).
3
3. State the position of Imam Abu Yusuf
On all three issues, Abu Yusuf holds that the seller cannot be compelled, and the present partner has no right to reclaim or withhold.
Abu Yusuf's view is a respected minority within the school.
4
4. Trace Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues that the present partner is a volunteer (mutabarri') when paying the absent partner's share. A volunteer cannot revoke, so he cannot reclaim. Since he is a stranger to the absent partner's share, he cannot compel the seller either.
Key assumption: paying without prior order is a gift.
5
5. Trace the reasoning of the two
The two argue that the present partner is compelled (majbur) because the transaction is a single bargain (safqa wahida). The seller can withhold the entire slave until full payment, preventing the present partner from benefiting from his share. Therefore, he is forced to pay the absent partner's share. A compelled payer has the right to reclaim and may withhold until repaid, analogous to the 'ariya case and agent's retention.
The illa (effective cause) is necessity due to indivisibility of the contract.

اشرف البدایہ جلد - نمیم
بائع کو شریک غائب کا پورا حصہ قبول کرنے پر مجبور کیا جا سکتا ہے یا نہیں۔ طرفین کے نزدیک شریک غائب کا حصہ شن قبول کرنے پر بائع کو مجبور کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک مجبور نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ -۲- اگر بائع نے شریک غائب کا حصہ ثمن قبول کر لیا تو کیا بائع کو اس بات پر مجبور کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ وہ شریک حاضر کو غائب کا حصہ بیع سپرد کرے یا اس پر مجبور نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے۔ طرفین کے نزدیک تو Chapter of Salam
84
Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume Nine
Can the seller be compelled to accept the entire share of the absent partner? According to the two, the seller can be compelled to accept the share of the absent partner. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf, he cannot be compelled.
-2- If the seller accepted the price of the absent partner’s share, can the seller be compelled to hand over the absent partner’s share to the present partner? According to the two, he can be compelled, but according to Imam Abu Yusuf, he cannot be compelled. Rather, the present partner will only take possession of his own share, and taking possession of his own share means that he will take service from that slave on his own days, and will not take service on the absent partner’s days.
3- If the present partner took possession of the slave and paid the full price, does the present partner have the right to reclaim the half price paid from the absent partner? According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the present partner does not have this right, and according to the two, he has this right. Rather, the present partner also has the right to withhold the absent partner’s share of the sold item until the absent partner pays his share of the price, so that when the absent partner pays his share of the price, the present partner will hand over his share of the sold item to him.
The proof of Imam Abu Yusuf is that the present partner, in paying the debt of the absent partner—i.e., the price—is a volunteer and a benefactor. And a volunteer does not have the right of recourse after volunteering. Therefore, the present partner will not have the right to reclaim half the price from the absent partner. And when the present partner does not have the right to reclaim half the price, he will also not have the right to take possession of the absent partner’s share of the sold item, because the present partner is a stranger with respect to the share of his absent partner, and no stranger has the right to take possession of another’s share. And when the present partner is not authorized to take possession of the absent partner’s share of the sold item, how can the seller be compelled to hand over the absent partner’s share of the sold item? And since the present partner, in paying half the price on behalf of the absent partner, is a volunteer, and no one is compelled to accept voluntary gifts, therefore the seller will not be compelled to accept the absent partner’s share of the price.
The proof of the two is that the present partner is helpless and compelled in paying the full price, because the present partner cannot derive benefit from his share until he pays the full price. This is because the transaction is one (single bargain), and the seller can withhold the sold item as long as even a small portion of the price remains owed by the buyer. So if the present partner paid his share of the price and did not pay the absent partner’s share, then since the sale is a single bargain, the seller will have the right to withhold the entire sold item, i.e., the slave. And when in this case the seller has the right to withhold the entire sold item, it becomes impossible for the present partner to derive benefit from his own share. And when without paying the price on behalf of the absent partner, it is impossible for the present partner to derive benefit from his share, the present partner becomes compelled and helpless to pay the absent partner’s share of the price on his behalf. And a person who, being compelled and constrained, pays something on behalf of another without his order, that constrained person has the right to reclaim the property paid. For example, a person gave something as a loan for use ('ariya) to another so that he could pawn it with someone to obtain a loan and conduct his business. So the borrower (musta'mir) pawned it with someone and took a loan. Then the borrower (i.e., the pledgor) became insolvent or disappeared, and the lender (mu'ir, owner of the pawned item) paid the debt of the mortgagee and redeemed his item. Then the lender can reclaim from the pledgor the property that he paid on his behalf to the mortgagee, even though the lender paid without the order of the pledgor, because the lender is compelled to pay the debt, and he is compelled because he cannot derive benefit from his property until he pays the debt of the mortgagee. Similarly, in the mentioned issue, since the present partner was compelled in paying the absent partner’s share of the price on his behalf, therefore he was given the right to reclaim it from the absent partner. And when the right of recourse is established for the present partner, he will also have the right to withhold the sold item from the absent partner until he recovers his full right, just as if a person appointed another as an agent to purchase something, and the agent paid the price from his own wealth, then this agent can withhold the sold item from the principal until he recovers his full price.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Ikhtilaf) between the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, referred to as 'طرفین') and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the rights of a present partner (sharik hadir) who buys a slave jointly with an absent partner (sharik gha'ib). The dispute unfolds in three sub-issues: (1) whether the seller can be compelled to accept the absent partner's share of the price, (2) whether the seller can be compelled to hand over the absent partner's share to the present partner, and (3) whether the present partner can reclaim the half-price paid on behalf of the absent partner and withhold the slave until repayment.

Imam Abu Yusuf argues that the present partner acts as a volunteer (mutabarri') when paying the absent partner's share, and a volunteer cannot revoke or reclaim after giving. Therefore, the seller cannot be compelled to accept the absent partner's share or hand it over, and the present partner has no right of recourse.

The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that the present partner is compelled (majbur), not a volunteer, because the transaction is a single bargain (safqah wahidah). The seller can withhold the entire slave until the full price is paid. If the present partner only pays his share, he cannot benefit from his share because the seller will retain the whole slave. Thus, he is forced to pay the absent partner's share to obtain benefit. A compelled person who pays on behalf of another without authorization has the right to reclaim. They support this with an analogy (qiyas): if someone lends an item for use ('ariya) with the understanding that the borrower will pawn it, and the borrower defaults, the lender who redeems the item can reclaim the paid amount from the borrower. Similarly, the present partner can reclaim the absent partner's share. Furthermore, like an agent who buys with his own money, the present partner can withhold the sold item until repaid.

The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to this text is that of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), as their argument is presented with an analogy and the text does not indicate a preference for Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
شریک حاضر (sharīk ḥāḍir): The partner who is present and makes the purchase, paying the full price.
شریک غائب (sharīk ghā'ib): The partner who is absent and did not participate in the purchase or payment.
مجبور (majbūr): Compelled by necessity; a person who pays on another's behalf because he cannot otherwise benefit from his own property.
مُتَبَرِّع (mutabarri'): A volunteer who pays without being under any legal compulsion; such a person cannot reclaim the payment.
صَفْقَة وَا حِدَة (ṣafqa wāḥida): A single bargain or transaction that cannot be split; the entire sale is considered one contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why does the present partner have the right to reclaim the absent partner's share of the price?
A) Because the present partner volunteered to pay and can change his mind.
B) Because the present partner was compelled to pay due to the single transaction, analogous to the 'ariya case.
C) Because the absent partner explicitly ordered him to pay.
D) Because the seller forced the present partner to pay the full price.

Key Takeaway
In joint purchases, a partner compelled to pay the full price due to a single bargain has the right to reclaim the absent partner's share and withhold the item until repaid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Purchase with Gold and Silver: Interpreting Ambiguous Price Terms
Ruling with proof on ambiguous price terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer uses the word 'mithqal' for both gold and silver, and apply the principle of customary weight (wazn ma'hud) when 'mithqal' is omitted.

Core Idea
When 'mithqal' is attributed equally, split half-and-half; otherwise, use customary weights.

In sales contracts, wording is critical. This lesson examines a case from Al-Hidayah where the buyer mentions both gold and silver in the price. How does the Hanafi school resolve the ambiguity? The answer hinges on whether the word 'mithqal' is explicitly stated and how it is attributed to each commodity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) buyer says 'a thousand mithqal of gold and silver', (2) buyer says 'a thousand of gold and silver' without 'mithqal'.
The second case is cut off at the end of the page but the principle is given.
2
Analyze the attribution of 'mithqal'
In the first case, 'mithqal' is directly attributed to both gold and silver equally. This triggers the rule of equal attribution.
The Arabic phrase 'addafa al-mithqal ilayhima 'ala al-sawa'' is key.
3
Apply the principle of no preference
Even though mithqal is customarily associated with gold, silver has a competing preference (common use in transactions). The preferences cancel out, leaving equal attribution.
The objection and rebuttal illustrate the balancing of 'urf.
4
Derive the ruling for the first case
Since no preference outweighs, the price is split: 500 mithqal of gold and 500 mithqal of silver.
The sale is sahih (valid).
5
Apply the second case principle
When 'mithqal' is absent, the price refers to the customary weight unit for each: mithqals for gold, dirhams (of weight seven) for silver.
This relies on the Hanafi principle of wazn ma'hud.

ایک باندی ہزار مثقال سونے اور چاندی کے عوض خریدی تو یہ دونوں نصف نصف لازم ہوں گے
قال ومن اشترى جارية بالف مثقال ذهب وفضة فهما نصفان لانه اضاف المثقال اليهما على السواء فيجب من كل واحد منهما خمس مائة مثقال لعدم الأولوية وبمثله لو اشترى جارية بالف من الذهب والفضة يجب من الذهب مثاقيل ومن الفضة دراهم وزن سبعة لانه اضاف الالف اليهما فينصرف إلى الوزن المعهود في كل واحد منهما
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک باندی ایک ہزار مثقال سونے اور چاندی کے عوض خریدی تو یہ دونوں نصف نصف لا زم ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ اس نے مثقال کو ان دونوں کی طرف یکساں منسوب کیا ہے۔ پس ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے پانچ سو مثقال واجب ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ وجہ اولویت نہیں ہے۔ اور اس کے مثل اگر کسی نے سونے اور چاندی سے ایک ہزار کے عوض باندی خریدی تو سونے سے مثقال واجب ہوں گے۔ اور چاندی سے وزن سبعہ کے دراہم راجب ہوں گے۔ اسلئے کہ اس نے ہزار کو ان دونوں کی طرف منسوب کیا ہے۔ لہذا ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک میں وزن معہود کی طرف رجوع ہوگا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے ایک باندی خریدی اور یہ کہا میں نے اس باندی کو ایک ہزار مثقال سونے اور چاندی کے عوض خریدا ہے تو یہ بیچ صحیح ہے اور مشتری پر پانچ سو مثقال سونے کے واجب ہوں گے اور پانچ سو مثقال چاندی کے واجب ہوں گے اور اگر یہ اعتراض کیا جائے کہ سونے کے مثاقبل کو ترجیح حاصل ہے کیونکہ مثقال کے ساتھ سونے کا اختصاص ہے۔ بایں طور کہ مشاقیل کے ذریعہ سونے کا وزن کیا جاتا ہے چاندی کا وزن نہیں کیا جاتا تو اس ترجیح کی وجہ سے پورے ایک ہزار مثقال سونے کے واجب ہونے چاہئیں۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جس طرح اس اعتبار سے سونے کو ترجیح حاصل ہے اسی طرح دوسرے اعتبار سے چاندی کو تر جیح حاصل ہے۔ بایں طور کہ بیچ وغیرہ میں بالعموم چاندی کا استعمال ہوتا ہے۔ سونے کا استعمال نہیں ہوتا۔ پس جب دونوں میں نے ہر ایک کو ایک ایک وجہ ترجیح حاصل ہے تو تعارض کی وجہ سے دونوں و جنہیں ترجیح ساقط ہو گئیں اور جب دونوں ساقط ہو گئیں تو سونے اور چاندی دونوں کی طرف مثقال کی نسبت برابر ہونے کی وجہ سے دونوں کے نصف نصف مثقال واجب ہوں گے یعنی پانچ سو مثقال سونے کے اور پانچ سو مثقال چاندی کے واجب ہوں گے۔ دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے ایک ہزار سونے اور چاندی کے عوض باندی
If a slave girl is purchased for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver, then both are half and half obligatory.
He said: And whoever buys a slave girl for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver, they are half and half, because he attributed 'mithqal' to both equally, so from each one five hundred mithqal are obligatory due to lack of preference. And similarly, if he bought a slave girl for a thousand of gold and silver, then from gold mithqals are obligatory, and from silver dirhams of weight seven are obligatory, because he attributed 'alif' (thousand) to both, so it refers to the customary weight in each one.
Translation: And if someone bought a slave girl for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver, then both are half and half obligatory. Because he attributed mithqal to both equally. So from each of them, five hundred mithqal are obligatory due to lack of preference. And similarly, if someone bought a slave girl for a thousand of gold and silver, then from gold mithqals are obligatory, and from silver dirhams of weight seven are obligatory, because he attributed 'thousand' to both, so it refers to the customary weight in each one.
Explanation: The case is that a person bought a slave girl and said, 'I bought this slave girl for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver,' then this sale is valid, and the buyer owes five hundred mithqal of gold and five hundred mithqal of silver. And if it is objected that gold mithqal has preference because mithqal is specifically for gold, in that gold is weighed by mithqals and silver is not weighed by mithqals, then due to this preference, the entire thousand should be gold. The answer is that just as gold has preference in this respect, similarly silver has preference in another respect: in sales and transactions, silver is commonly used, not gold. So when each has one ground of preference, they conflict and both preferences fall away. When both are removed, the attribution of mithqal to both becomes equal, so half and half become obligatory, i.e., five hundred mithqal of gold and five hundred mithqal of silver. The second case: if someone bought a slave girl for a thousand of gold and silver...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) concerning the interpretation of ambiguous price terms when both gold and silver are mentioned in a sale. The author presents two cases.

First case: If the buyer says 'for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver' (with the word 'mithqal' explicitly applied to both), then the price is split equally: 500 mithqal of gold and 500 mithqal of silver. The reasoning is that 'mithqal' is equally attributed to both, and there is no preference (awlawiyyah) for one over the other. An objection is raised that mithqal is customarily used for gold, so the entire thousand should be gold. The answer: gold has a specific association with mithqal, but silver has a general usage in transactions. Since both have a competing claim to preference, the preferences cancel out, and the attribution becomes equal.

Second case: If the buyer says 'for a thousand of gold and silver' (without the word 'mithqal'), then the interpretation follows the customary weight (wazn ma'hud) for each: gold is measured in mithqals, silver in dirhams of weight seven (i.e., standard dirhams of 7 mithqal weight? Actually, it's dirhams of a specific weight, but the text says 'weight seven' meaning the standard dirham). So the buyer owes mithqals of gold and dirhams of silver, not a fixed number of coins.

The passage concludes by noting that the sale is valid (sahih) and the obligations are determined accordingly. The second case is cut off at the end of the page, but the ruling is complete for the first case and the principle for the second.

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqāl): A unit of weight, specifically used for gold; here it implies the standard mithqal weight of gold.
وزن معہود (wazn ma'hūd): The customary and well-known weight unit for each commodity: mithqal for gold, dirham for silver.
دراہم وزن سبعہ (darāhim wazn sab'a): Dirhams of weight seven, i.e., silver coins weighing seven mithqals (or specifically, the standard dirham weight in Hanafi law).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says, 'I buy this slave girl for a thousand mithqal of gold and silver,' what is the price according to the Hanafi school?
A) 1000 mithqal of gold only, because mithqal is specific to gold.
B) 500 mithqal of gold and 500 mithqal of silver.
C) 1000 mithqal of silver only, because silver is commonly used in transactions.
D) The sale is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ambiguous price terms are resolved by equal attribution when a weight term is equally applied, and by customary weight when the weight term is omitted.
