The Definition of Sarf: Linguistic and Technical Meaning
Defines sarf linguistically and technically via a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic meaning of sarf, explain its technical meaning as a contract of gold and silver exchange, and identify why the contract is named 'sarf' based on the element of excess.

Core Idea
Sarf is named for the excess it seeks, because gold and silver have no intrinsic benefit.

This passage introduces the term 'sarf' from two angles: its linguistic root meaning 'excess' and its technical application as a contract for exchanging gold and silver. The author connects these meanings by arguing that the contract inherently requires seeking a material gain (excess) because the metals themselves yield no direct utility. Understanding this link is essential for grasping the unique nature of the sarf contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
In the hadith referenced, 'sarf' is used for supererogatory (nafl) worship, which is 'excess' over obligatory acts. Thus the core linguistic meaning is 'excess' or 'extra'.
2
Identify the technical meaning
Technically, sarf is a contract for the exchange of gold and silver for gold and silver. It is a specific type of sale with strict conditions.
3
Understand the rationale linking the two meanings
Gold and silver have no intrinsic benefit usufruct (like grains for hunger or clothing for covering). Therefore, their trade must aim for a material excess (e.g., exchanging impure for pure, unminted for minted) to avoid futility. Hence the contract is named after the concept of 'excess'.
4
Apply the definition to distinguish sarf from other sales
In ordinary sales, benefit is derived from the substance itself. In sarf, benefit is purely through excess in value. This distinction justifies the special rules (like immediate possession and equality) that Hanafi fiqh imposes on sarf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اس حدیث میں لفظ صرف عبادت نافلہ کیلئے استعمال کیا گیا ہے اور عبادت نافلہ بھی فرائض سے زائد ایک چیز ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ صرف کے معنی زیادتی کے ہیں اور عقد صرف سے مطلوب بھی زیادتی ہوتی ہے۔ کیونکہ سونا اور چاندی دونوں ایسی چیزیں ہیں جن کی ذات سے کوئی نفع نہیں اٹھایا جاتا ہے۔ اسکے بر خلاف اناج، کپڑا اور گدھا وغیرہ کہ ان کی ذات سے نفع اٹھایا جاتا ہے۔ اس طور پر کہ اناج سے پیٹ بھرتا ہے اور کپڑے سے ستر کا کام لیا جاتا ہے اور گدھا سواری کے کام میں آتا ہے۔ پس جب سونے اور چاندی کی ذات غیر مشتقع بہ ہیں تو ان کی تجارت سے مقصور زیادتی کو طلب کرنا ہوگا۔ بایں طور کہ کھرے کے بدلے میں کھوٹا دےدے یا بغیر ڈھلے ہوئے کے بدلے میں ڈھلا ہوالے لے ۔ اسلئے کہ اگر ان کی تجارت سے زیادتی بھی مطلوب نہ ہو تو عقد صرف فائدہ سے خالی ہوگا۔ حالانکہ عقد کرنے سے عاقدین کا مقصود کوئی نہ کوئی فائدہ ضرور ہوتا ہے۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ عقد صرف سے زیادتی مطلوب ہوتی ہے اور صرف کے لغوی معنی بھی چونکہ زیادتی کے ہیں۔ اسلئے اس عقد کا نام صرف رکھا گیا ہے۔
In this hadith, the word 'sarf' has been used for supererogatory worship, and supererogatory worship is also something additional over the obligatory duties. So it became known that the meaning of 'sarf' is excess, and what is sought from the contract of sarf is also excess. Because gold and silver are both things from which no benefit is derived from their substance. Unlike grains, cloth, and a donkey, etc., from which benefit is derived from their substance: grains fill the stomach, cloth is used for covering, and a donkey is used for riding. So when the substance of gold and silver is not intrinsically beneficial, then from their trade, excess must be sought, such as giving impure (low quality) in exchange for pure (high quality), or taking minted (coin) in exchange for unminted. Because if excess were not sought from their trade, the contract of sarf would be devoid of benefit, despite the fact that by contracting, the contracting parties must intend some benefit. So it is established that excess is sought from the contract of sarf, and since the linguistic meaning of 'sarf' is also excess, therefore this contract is named 'sarf'.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sarf' both linguistically and technically. The author begins by referring to a hadith where the word 'sarf' is used for supererogatory prayer (nafl), establishing the linguistic meaning of 'excess' or 'extra'. He then argues that the contract of sarf (exchange of gold and silver) inherently seeks excess (ziyadah), because gold and silver have no intrinsic utility unlike food, clothing, or animals. Their only benefit is through exchange, and to avoid a contract being futile, the parties must intend a gain, such as exchanging a less pure or unminted item for a better or minted one. Thus the contract is named 'sarf' because it involves an excess.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): In this text, sarf is a contract for the exchange of gold and silver for gold and silver, requiring equality in weight and immediate possession. Linguistically it means excess.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it requires immediate payment

Key Takeaway
The name 'sarf' for gold/silver exchange derives from the linguistic meaning 'excess', reflecting the necessary gain in such transactions.

Equality of Weight in Gold and Silver Exchange
States ruling and proof for gold/silver exchange equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging gold for gold (or silver for silver) and identify the two hadith proofs that require equality in weight and immediate possession.

Core Idea
Gold for gold must be equal weight, hand to hand; any excess is riba, quality irrelevant.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a fundamental rule of Islamic finance: when exchanging gold (or silver) for the same metal, the weights must be exactly equal, and the exchange must be completed on the spot. Even differences in purity or craftsmanship do not permit any imbalance. The ruling is rooted in two prophetic hadiths that form the basis of riba al-fadl (usury of surplus) in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding this principle is essential for any valid transaction of precious metals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling silver for silver or gold for gold. The ruling: it is not permissible unless equal for equal.
The default ruling is impermissibility; equality is a condition for validity.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites two hadiths: (1) 'Gold for gold, like for like, weight for weight, hand to hand; any excess is riba.' (2) 'Its good and its bad are the same.'
First hadith establishes equality and immediate possession; second removes quality as a factor.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'mithlan bi mithl' indicates exact equivalence in weight; 'fadl riba' proves that any surplus is prohibited riba. The second hadith equates good and bad quality, preventing any value adjustment.
The prohibition is based on the word 'riba' in the hadith.
4
Note the conditions
Two conditions are deduced: equality in weight (or measure for measured items) and immediate possession (qabd) in the contracting session.
Both must be present; absence of either renders the contract fasid.
5
Recognize the scope
This ruling applies only when the genus is the same (gold for gold). Different genera (e.g., gold for silver) are governed by different rules.
The commentary clarifies that the six commodities mentioned are ribawi.

Selling silver for silver, gold for gold, equal for equal, is permissible. He (the author) said: If someone sells silver for silver or gold for gold, it is not permissible except equal for equal, even if they differ in quality and workmanship, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Gold for gold, equal for equal, weight for weight, hand to hand; and any excess is riba.' And he (peace be upon him) said: 'Its good and its bad are the same.' And we have mentioned it in the Book of Sales. Translation: If someone sells silver in exchange for silver, or gold in exchange for gold, it is not permissible except equal for equal, even if they differ in fineness and minting, because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Gold in exchange for gold, equal for equal, weight for weight, hand to hand, and excess is riba.' And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Their good and their bad are the same.' And we have mentioned this in the Book of Sales. Explanation: The ruling is that if a person sells silver for silver or gold for gold, this contract is valid only when the two items are equal in weight, even if they differ in quality or workmanship. For example, one item is fine and minted while the other is less fine and unminted. In short, even if the two items differ in quality or workmanship, equality is necessary for the validity of the contract. The author of Hidayah presents two hadiths as proof. One: The Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned six things—gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt—and said: 'Like for like, weight for weight, measure for measure, hand to hand, and excess is riba.' That is, he mandated two things: equality in weight for weighed items and in measure for measured items, and possession of the items in the contracting session. If equality by weight or measure is not observed, or possession is not taken in the session, then it is riba and forbidden. This hadith proves that equality is a condition in the sale of ribawi items. The second hadith is that the good and bad of ribawi items are the same. That is, if one item is good and the other is bad, equality is still necessary, and excess is forbidden. From this hadith it is understood that Islamic law did not consider the difference between good and bad; rather, it accorded both equal status.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that when exchanging gold for gold or silver for silver, the two items must be equal in weight, even if their quality differs. The author quotes two hadiths: the famous hadith of the six commodities (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) which require equality and immediate hand-to-hand transfer; and the hadith that the good and bad are the same, meaning quality does not affect the requirement of equality. The commentary explains that the contract is only valid with equal weight, and any excess whether in weight or in value due to workmanship constitutes riba. The passage also introduces the condition of possession (qabd) in the same session, which will be elaborated in the next section.

Key Terms
ربوا (riba): In this passage, riba refers to any excess in the exchange of ribawi items when equality is not maintained.
مثلاً بمثل (mithlan bi mithl): Equality in weight or measure, used for gold/silver and other weighed/measured commodities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what is the ruling if someone sells gold for gold with an extra weight of gold?
A) It is permissible if the quality is higher
B) It is not permissible because any excess is riba
C) It is permissible if both parties agree
D) It is permissible if the transaction is immediate

Key Takeaway
When exchanging the same ribawi commodity (gold for gold, silver for silver), strict equality in weight and immediate hand-to-hand transfer are obligatory; any deviation renders the contract riba and invalid.
